THE BRONTE FAMILY.
There was surely never a better illustration of the
generalisation which modern pathologists approach
—the theory which links genius, insanity, intem-
perance, and crime as modes of physical degenera-
tion—than the history of the Bronte family. The
father, though a clergyman, was nothing short of
a murderer —through ignorance or neglect of the
laws of the body. A lusty Irishman, with
insane tendencies, he married a young Oornish-
woman, who seems, though delicate, to have been
normally healthy; and killed her by abuse —if
there is any truth in physiology. The unfortu-
nate creature gave birth to six children in seven
years, and died of physical exhaustion. The
family were all weak and unhealthy, and the son
was vicious. All were brilliantly clever. None
lived to pass middle-age.
# # «
The brutal moral is obvious. To get a genius,
mate a vigorous man, with tainted blood, to a
high-strung nervous woman—or vice versd —and
produce, a dozen children in a dozen years. If
half of them live over puberty, you will certainly
have a genius—perhaps two or three. The more
closely you can tauten these conditions to break-
ing strain—the more nerve-disease you can get
into your subjects without killing them off too
soon —the greater your genius is likely to be.
• » •
The young wife was brought from “ the warm
Cornish sea that was her home”—brought to live
in a parsonage overlooking a graveyard on a bleak
and lonely Yorkshire moor ; and the water for the
use of the family seems to have been obtained from
wells into which the pollution of the graveyard
filtered ! The crazy, tyrannical father always
carried a loaded pistol, which he fired off at ran-
dom when his temper was bad. In this inhospit-
able environment the children withered and died
one by one.
• # •
Maria Branwell (Mrs. Bronte) was married in
1812. She died in 1821, in her 39th year. The
children were :
Maria, died 1825, aged 11.
Elizabeth ~ 1825 ~ 10.
Patrick „ 1848 „ 31.
Emily „ 1848 „ 30.
Anne „ 1849 „ 29.
Charlotte,, 1855 ~ 39.
What a sacrifice ! And the old father, born in
1777, outlived them all. Truly, his life was nour-
ished on their blood !
* * m
The series of Bronte novels is one of the
si.l ongest in fiction. Compare them with Lytton’s,
■or example, and you get at its keenest the strik-
ing contrast between genius and talent. Lytton’s
works are constructed by rule and measure accord-
ing to the best principles of art. They charm and
attract, but rarely move or influence. Charlotte
Bronte, the chief of the three sisters who lived to
write, has a much more limited scope, much less
learning and skill; but her passionate force is
irresistible. Lytton’s characters are only figures
in a picture—correctly drawn, admirably colored,
and arranged according to the best theories of
proportion and perspective. Charlotte Bronte’s
live. They are real personages—we are convinced
of it—painted with the writer’s life-blood, utter-
ing with stress and pang the thoughts which
agitate and overwhelm her.
# * " #
“Jane Byre ” is generally considered Charlotte
Bronte’s best work ; and for variety of power it
takes first place. It is not, however, so har-
monious or so attractive as “The Professor,”
which has more concentrated interest than either
“Jane Eyre,” “Yiilette,” or “Shirley.” It is
noteworthy (did not Lewes first make the
remark ?) that C.B. is least successful when purely
imaginative. Given the smallest base of realism
—some fact, some character she knows—and she
can deduce an endless series of consequences and
characteristics not actually perceived, but vividly
and correctly imagined. Without some actual,
sensible knowledge, her imagination robs her of
verisimilitude and coherence. The force of her
work lies rather in intensity than in extent; her
characters are all kindred, her heroes and heroines
have a striking family likeness.
* # #
Charlotte Bronte had a marvellous power of
observation and analysis, high dramatic talent,
and a gift of keen and glowing language. Hence
she could see the most commonplace personages as
under a magnifying-glass, in all their marvellous
humanity ; and so paint them to the unobservant
multitude. Her insight and executive force
together made her genius.
* * •
Her passion for the outre was a sign of heredi-
tary taint. The normal man loves the normal
woman ; the normal woman the normal man. The
abnormal in either sex—the degenerates of genius,
drink, insanity, or crime—gloat on irregularity,
monstrosity. Hence the perverted instinct of
sexual criminals. Victor Hugo has painted.some-
thing of this in Duchess Josiane. “ Les hommes,
fi done ! un dieu tout au plus etait digne d’elle,
on un monstre.” Doubtless C.B.’s female heroines
display her own character, likes and dislikes.
# # #
“The Professor” and “Yiilette” embody
Charlotte’s experience as a governess in Brussels.
Both are good and clever, interesting and true.
But Shirley is C.B.’s most fascinating character.
The love-scenes with Louis are superb. The force
of genius is in the dialogue: it is like Dumas at
bis best. The rest of the book, though exceedingly
good, is rather too sketchy—there is not sufficient
plot for the incidents, no central thread strong
enough to bind so many gems.
* * #
“ Wuthering Heights,” by Emily Bronte, is an
extraordinary 1 book—crude, without proportion,
yet not without power. There is a good deal of
dramatic talent, but small literary talent; all is
realism, nothing imagination ; and the course of
the narrative is confined. “Agnes Grey,” by
Anne Bronte, describes the experiences of a
governess ; and is graphic enough to interest. It
ends with the heroine’s marriage to a clergyman ;
and three children make home happy. Poor
Anne !
* * *
“ The Tenant of Wildfell Hall,” also by A. 8.,
is a conscientious story intended to show “ the
evils of drink,” and is as dull as most stories with
a purpose. Yet there is a naive truth in all the
Bronte sisters write which makes their books
interesting—close observers and analysts as the
three girls were. The poems by Charlotte, Emily
and Anne are not of first-class merit; and Emily’s
are the best. The others are hardly more than
schoolgirl rhymes, though showing more than
schoolgirlish vigor of thought and language. Mrs.
Gaskell’s “Life of C. 8.” is a tame production,
suppressing a great deal that clashes with “ re-
spectable” notions ; but the interest of the subject
shines through in despite.
The Bronte Novels.
In a neat and uniform pocket edition, in
half-cloth, clearly printed, the works of the
Bronte sisters and Mrs. Gaskell’s “Life”
(7 vols., posted for 12s. 6d.), are one of the
best current investments in English litera-
ture. Single vols., Is lOd each, as under :

Grev.

Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.

Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is 3d. The anchor’s
best book, well printed.

markable of Russian realistic novels.

and stories.

Paper, 9d. The bane or the antidote.

lOd. Delightfully cynical.

and altogether lovely.

4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying

clever style at the back of all.

The Odd Number. Thirteen stories by Guy
de Maupassant, a choice selection (chiefly
from the “Contes de Lundi ”), and ex-
hibiting a side of the author’s genius not

listed.
A Saint, and other tales, by Paul Bourget.
The “other tales” are “A Gambler,”
“Two Little Boys,” and “Childhood
Perfidy’—all characteristic of the author’s
limpid method.
The Crime of Sylvestre Bonnard, by Anatole
France. '
Dame Care, by .Hermann Sudermann.
The House by the Medlar-Tree, by Giovanni
Yerga.
Gallegher, and other stories, by Richard
Harding Davis.
Balaam and his Master, and other sketches
and stories, by J oel Chandler Harris.
With the doubtful exception of “The
Odd Number,” the stories in this series
are rather recommended by style and
sympathy than by plot or vivid interest.
Davis and Harris have considerable Ameri-
can vogue, justified by their drollery and
perception ; but the average reader finds
them tending to dullness. Yerga, Suder-
mann, and Anatole France employ the
charm of simplicity, not the fascination of
strength and splendour. Bourget’s stories
are lilies, not roses of literature—unless it
be white moss-roses. De Maupassant’s
striking book can be recommended for
general purchase : the others require some
“ acquired taste,” some welcome of appre-
ciative culture. Paper, 3s. each.

Q URSELVES!
We wish It to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept Knall profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
gomewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY,
' The Bulletin’s ’ Scrap-Book.
ENVY.
He was the first always. Fortune
Shone bright in his face.
I fought for years ; with no effort
He conquered the place.
We ran—my feet were all bleeding,
But he won the race.
Spite of his many successes,
Men loved him the same;
My one pale ray of good fortune
Met scoffing and blame.
When we erred they gave him pity,
But me—only shame.
My home was still in the shadow,
His lay in the sun ;
I longed in vain ; what he asked for
It straightway was done.
Once I staked all my heart’s treasure —
We played—and he won.
Yes, and just now I have seen him
Cold, smiling and blest,
Laid in his coffin—God help me !
While he is at rest
I am cursed still to live —
Even Death loved him best.
HOUSEHOLD WORDS.
IF YOU LOVE ME, TELL ME SO.
If yon love me, tell me so ;
Wait not till the Summer glow
Fades in Autumn’s changeful light,
Amber clouds and purple night.
Wait not till the Summer hours
Heap with snow-drifts all the flowers,
Till the tide of life runs low— •
If you love me, tell me so !
If you love me, tell me so,
While the river’s dreamy flow
Holds the love-enchanted hours
Steeped in music, crowned with flowers ;
Ere the Summer’s dreamy days
Fade in Autumn’s mystic haze—
Ere is hushed the music flow—
If you love me, tell me so.
If you love me, tell me so ;
Let me hear the sweet words low )
Let me know while life is fair,
Feel your kisses on my hair.
While in womanhood’s first bloom,
Ere shall come dark hours of gloom,
In the first fresh dawning glow—
If you love me, tell me so !

A Westralian log lately cut was 68ft. long,
and 13ft. lOin. mean girth ; actual cubic contents,
10,632 super, feet; estimated weight, 30 tons
(including bark). A 27ft. length was cut off the
butt of the tree, then the 58ft. length, and then a
30ft. length. It is questioned whether another log
of such size has ever been cut in any Australian
sawmill.

Dear Bulletin, — In a recent exhaustive
description of Rockhampton (Q.) in Sydney Mail
it is casually stated that the inhabitants are in no
hurry for railway connection with the South.
This is mild ; but it would scarcely suit the Fat-
man of these parts to have the danger pointed out
in more emphatic terms. Look at the facts—and
the map. Rockhampton owes its very existence
to the central railway-line which stretches more
than half across the province. It is the artery
which supplies Rockhampton with its life. Tap
it fully at any point near the coast and the pro-
sperous city starts to slide downward to ruin.
Political influence and the constant strain for
Central Separation can only ward off the danger
for a little while. When the railway commis-
sioners decided that the disconnected section of
the line from Bundaberg to Gladstone was to lie
rusting and rotting through the bush, Rock-
hampton breathed more freely, but the trouble is
merely postponed. It is Gladstone which menaces,
with every natural advantage over the newer city.
Rockhampton lies on a flat about 34 miles from
the sea up a shallow and tortuous river. Over
.£160,000 has been expended on the tricky channel
and a yearly fortune is needed to maintain a pas-
sage. Nearly the whole of Rockhampton cargo is
transhipped on the open waters of Keppel Bay to
the small tenders which take the goods up the
river. The river is practically unnavigable at
night. A little further south lies the fine harbour
of Port Curtis with the apathetic town of Glad-
stone on its deep-water shores. Sooner or later
Gladstone will connect by railway with either
Rockhampton or Westwood—no matter which ;
and then the few wooden inhabitants of this old
town will be bustled into life by the transferred
trade of the great Central line, while the city of
sin, sweat, and sorrow will dwindle into insignifi-
cance.—Jackbird.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply ns with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THiaiEJIEJ CASTLES ” and “ O^-IPST-A-HST.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore; ippeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere .
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal : —The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :—li. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, JUNE 15, 1895.
The “Tote.”
One great cause of the world’s universal
poverty is the vast proportion of useless
non-producers which it- contains, and the
coinpar-atively small proportion of producers.
And, in speaking thus, it is necessary to draw
a sharp line of demarcation between useless
and useful non-producers. The producer, in
the hard-and-fast sense of certain Labor
representatives, is merely the man who sup-
plies the world with food, clothing and
lodging, and a few other bare necessaries of
a plain, gaunt, unpoetic existence the
horny son of toil in his hardest, most horny,
and most restricted aspect. Everyone
else is the non-producer, and is, consequently,
useless and objectionable. But as man
“ lives not by bread alone,” the undeniable
logic of facts makes this simple and beautiful
definition impossible. The artist, the actor,
singer, sculptor, architect, musician, and
writer are all non-producers in a sense, and
all persons whom the world could do with-
out, but if they were abolished or left out of
the scheme of creation, existence would be
so long and lank and utterly melancholy that
it would cease to be worth having. The
middleman, in all his varieties, commercial
and financial, is not actually a producer, and
he has multiplied far—very far—beyond the
world’s requirements, till he has become
a burden and a parasite ; still, in some
sense, and to a certain degree, he is
indispensable. And among others of the
much-contemned non-producing class are
physicians, scientists of all kinds, teachers,
lawyers, parsons, magistrates, Judges, police-
men, legislators, monarchs, civil-servants,
seamen, pawnbrokers, and, in short, almost
the whole of the town and city population
generally. Some of them are mere diseases
—equally useless and objectionable. Pro-
bably three-fourths of the great army of
merchants, dealers, importers, brokers,
agents, shop-keepers and hawkers could be
dispensed with, and the community be all
the better for their absence. The value of
the parson, as he is at present constituted, is
wholly problematical. The lawyer is one-
half the cause and one-half the effect of the
confusion and injustice of the law. The
civil-servant is partly a valuable and essen-
tial member of the community, and partly
an elegant pauper who is billeted on the
country through the influence of his uncle or
some other relative. The non-producing
classes, in short, are, to some degree, the
highest product of civilisation, and, for the
rest, they are an epitome of laziness, fraud
and sin, and to bracket them all together in
one unreasoning malediction is both foolish
and illogical.
Nevertheless, in one sense they are alike.
The actual producer has to support them all,
and the larger their proportion the heavier
becomes the struggle of life to those at the
bottom. Therefore, whoever can invent a
method by which even one non-producer is
abolished, without injury to anything in our
civilisation that is worth preserving, is a
benefactor to his species. And this exordium
brings The Bulletin at last to the subject of
the present article. During the session of
1895, bills to legalise the totalisator in Vic-
toria and New South Wales will be discussed
in the legislatures of those provinces. In
each case the measure is in the hands of a
private member ; and the want of Govern-
ment support, the indifference of many
legislators, and the unholy alliance which
has been formed against the proposal by the
snufflebusters of the community, the be-
jewelled A aeons and Cohens, the sporting
tobacconists, the fraudulent “ tote’’-owners,
and the riff-raff of the racecourses generally,
makes up a strong opposition. Under these
conditions the chances in favor of the Bill
appear decidedly small. Nevertheless, the
legalisation of the totalisator—as a scheme for
the abolition of one non-producing class —is
a move which ought to receive the most
cordial endorsement of all democrats, like-
wise of the Church, the respectable sporting
community, and all the supporters of law-
and-order generally. It should be on the
Labor programme, the Single-tax platform
and the Socialist platform, and be included
in the official policies of Freetraders, Protec-
tionists, prohibitionists, and all other parties,
divisions, factions and scrag-ends of political
humanity. And this paper respectfully
requests all these to give the proposal a fair
hearing, and to help it through in the
interests, not of party but of patriotism.
• # *
The opposition of the pious and pro-
foundly respectable section of the com-
munity is based on three axioms : (1) The
legal establishment of the “tote” is a
recognition of gambling ; (2) gambling is a
sin and a curse ; (3) therefore it should not
be recognised or permitted in any shape or
form. The first assertion is certainly true,
and the second is true, at least in part.
Gambling may or may not be a sin, but it
certainly is a curse. Unluckily, there is no
semblance of a probability that the curse is
going to depart from us. The Social Gimlet
section which demands that it should not be
regulated by law can do nothing to put it
down ; therefore it remains in an unregu-
lated state, and is, consequently, ten times
worse than it otherwise would be. Book-
making is nominally illegal, yet no jury,
magistrate or Judge will do anything of an
effective character to suppress it because the
sense of the community is not with the
Social Gimletists in the matter. “Totes”
are unlawful, yet there are a hundred or more
in Sydney alone, and no one will move in
real earnest to suppress them because the
great mass of the population doesn’t want
them suppressed. Three definite results,
however, have been achieved through the
impotent pretence of prohibition. The
totes are run secretly amid an atmosphere of
blasphemy, obscenity, bad tobacco-smoke
and whisky-breaths. They are all more
or less dishonest, and most of them
shamefully fraudulent, because no one
can sue a tote-keeper for the dividend that
is due to him—because the legal prohibition
naturally throws the work into the hands of
a low and shady class—and because no tote-
keeper can be honest and live, for he has to
buy immunity from the police and detectives
by secret bribes, and he can't make enough
money honestly to bribe enough police and
detectives to secure the aforesaid immunity.
And thus, as a third result, the
totes serve as means to corrupt the
police and detective force, and help to
make it so utterly untrustworthy as to be a
menace rather than a protection. Further,
the law as it now stands has increased the
the number of these fraudulent gambling
machines enormously. They are necessarily
run on a small scale and mostly in obscure
back-rooms, because it is not only necessary
to bribe the “force ” but to keep as much as
possible out of the sight of the Social Gimlet
Brigade which—despite its many faults—is
generally unpurchaseable. Therefore, as
there is a public demand for a vast amount
of gambling facilities, and as each fraudulent
tote-keeper can only supply a limited quan-
tity, the-number of these persons has grown
wonderfully and keeps on growing.
m * *
The bookmaker, the tote-keeper, the fan-
tan shark, and many others, are all evidences
of the great truth that man wants to gamble,
that he will gamble, and that while he feels
that way all the moral and constitutional
law on earth won’t stop him. The only
results of many years of legal repression are
that he gambles secretly amid objectionable
surroundings, that he doesn’t get anything
like fair value ?or his money, that he is a
considerable cost to the citato in abortive
prosecutions and the wages of police and
detectives, and that he gambles as much as
ever—perhaps more than ever. If repres
sion can do nothing better than this, then it
is time to try regulation instead. The fact
that regulation involves official recognition
amounts to nothing at all ; the evil exists,
and everyone knows that it exists, and there
is nothing more to be gained by refusing to re-
cognise its existence than there is by refusing
to recognise the existence of drink or disease
or death or Pahkes, or anything else that is
objectionable. Prohibition hasn’t diminished
the evil ; regulation can do no worse, and
will almost certainly do a lot better.
• • •
Thegambling “industryor, rather, this
one branch of it, for there are many others—
supports a very large number of persons.
The tote-keepers and their agents and
assistants, the bejewelled and diamond-
spangled bookmakers and their assistants
and hangers-on, number many hundreds.
They produce nothing, therefore the pro-
ducing classes have to support them all, and
to support them on a lavish scale. The
maintenance of this garish band costs very
many thousands per annum in actual cash,
and robs the country of many more
thousands which its members might produce
if forced to maintain themselves by repro-
ductive toil. If they and their industry
could be abolished together the saving to the
producing classes would be enormous. As
this particular form of gambling evidently
can’t be abolished, the next alternative is to
have the work conducted honestly and
openly, under proper supervision, and with
as few hands as possible, and the saving will
also be enormous. A legalised tote run at
each race-meeting under the control of the
various Jockey Clubs would extinguish the
great bulk of the bookmaking fraternity, sim-
ply because the machine gives better odds and
always pays up its losses. It would utterly
abolish the unlawful totes —nr 99 per cent,
of them—because the machine which doesn’t
require to pay bribes to police and detec-
tives, and to buy off informers, can always
give better returns than the one which does;
And with one or two totalisators working
openly, instead of a hundred lurking mys-
teriously down back streets, about one per
cent, of the labor would suffice; the crowd of
seedy loafers, hangers-on, blackmailers and
buttoners would evanish; and, of a large, use-
less, and utterly inartistic and depraved
class of non-producers, part w r ould be driven
to some form of profitable toil, some would
drift away to other countries, some would
die off in the congenial atmosphere of the
gaol, and among them all a great burden
would be lifted off the community.
• * «
These are the results which have been
accomplished, or are in process of accomplish-
ment, in those Australasian provinces where
the totalisator is already in operation. And,
in addition to them, the ma dune lias pro-
duced other advantages. With the decline
of the book making business the number of
glaring turf frauds has greatly diminished,
and a branch of sport which was growing
very foul indeed has become somewhat more
honest and reputable than hitherto. The
public gets better returns for its investments,
and the gambling disease is, therefore, less
ruinous, and creates less crime and misery
than it did aforetime. The Jockey Clubs
get a small percentage of the winnings, and
this source of income has enabled them not
only to improve their premises but to offer
largely increased stakes, which make it
worth the while of horse-owners to run for
prizes alone, instead of running to suit the
“ books.” And the Government gets another
small percentage which makes the machine
a source of revenue. In Maoriland tlie
Treasury receives between £lO,OOO and
£ll,OOO per annum from this source. In
Victoria or New South Wales it would prob-
ably net £20,000. All these matters com-
bined make a respectable aggregate reason
why the totalisator should be legalised under
proper conditions with all possible speed.
And, so far as The Bulletin knows, the
only reasons yet advanced against the inno-
vation are that it would destroy the profits
of the gaudy bookmaker—about the last in-
dividual who deserves consideration at the
hands of the public, and that it would be a
recognition of the vice, or alleged vice, of
gambling. And as that vice exists, whether
recognised or not, and exists all the more by
reason of its non-recognition, this latter plea
is hardly worth consideration.
• # •
This scheme for the reformation of turf-
gambling is not here put forward as a great
matter : on the contrary, it is a com-
paratively small one. The £20,000, or so,
per annum which Victoria and New South
Wales would each realise out of the State’s
percentage on totalisator receipts is not
much, though in these days of diminished
revenue and grinding economy even that
amount is not to be despised. Surpluses
nowadays—on these rare occasions when
there are any —have to be made up by pain-
fully collecting together all manner of small
sums, and no Government can afford to miss
even one little item of revenue, which can be
obtained without laying fresh burdens on the
people and thus accumulating unpopularity
for the next general election. Again, the
abolition of the many hundreds of utterly use-
less, and very costly non-producers who run
the present system of turf-gambling would
not be much of a relief to the pro-
ducing classes by whom they, and all other
unproductive workers or loafers, have to be
supported ; yet it would be a relief. Even
if one man who now does no useful work—
who does nut help to.enrich or refine or
enlighten or improve the community —call
be compelled to employ himself for a liveli-
hood in some occupation of value to the
nation, there is something gained. The
present unsatisfactory state of society can’t
he put right, by any one measure, no matter
how wise or beneficent it may be ; it bas to
be patched and amended by hundreds and
thousands of instalments, and no instal-
ment, however small, is to be despised.
Therefore The Bulletin urges that the
Totalisator Bill should not be killed by in-
difference because it is a comparatively
small matter, or sacrificed to superficial
snuffle as heretofore. The evil with which
it deals is only one among many, but it is a
very veal and urgent evil none the less.

The O’m of the Faddist.
“ Every paper gets, and prints, the letters it
deserves.” This is the sort of thing the Age
gives prominence to :
- PROTECTION V. LAND TAX.
To The Editor of The Age.
Sir, —We are told by Freetraders, Single-taxers, and
some misguided Protectionists that we should lower pro
lective duties, abolish revenue duties, and make up Vhe
difference by a land-tax. Are we not now making great
rejoicing over the fact that we are increasing our export
of natural products? Yet what is the result ? It is only
an experiment which must take Lime to prove its success
and develops into a proper undertaking. It does not pay
sufficiently now', or there w’ould be more capital and labor
employed in it. Yet what are our anti-Protectionists
trying to do? To kill our export-trade altogether. if
exporting beef, mutton, butter, &c., is not now suffi-
ciently remunerative to induce a flow of capital to such
industry, how will it be when a heavy impost is placed
upon land? Why, this will simply prevent any profit
being made out of such liade, and it will be abandoned.
If we pay for our imports by our exports, we must make
the conditions for exporting such that we may gain and
not lose by the transaction. Mr. Trbnwith anil others of
the Labor-party see this clearly enough, and it is lime
they discarded the land-tax cry which they have adopted,
I feel certain, because it has been made popular without
being understood. —Yours, &c.,
2Sth May. Protection Understood.
This is the shriek of the one-idead maniac—the
fatuous fanatic who goes about with a plank

the social problem is simple, not complex. On
the banks of Yarra, Melbourne may bear any
Sunday another saviour of his country who
declares that a diet of apples and nuts, com-
bined with deep-breathing, is the one certain,

Financial Responsibility.
Some particulars of the Victorian Companies
Bill, to he introduced this session by the Tur-
ner Government, are now available, and, so far
as they go, the measure looks promising. They
are summarised as follows :
The Bill makes it imperative that half the shares in a
company shall be subscribed for and half the subscribed
capital paid before it can he registered. Actions for the
recovery of forfeited snares must he commenced within
one year, directors may purchase forfeited shares for a
company at the amount of the due call, no liability com-
panies are pronibited from holding shares in another com-
pany, the directors or officers of a no-liability company
receiving money on deposit are personally liable for the
amount, but the liability will not pass to executors ; com-
panies and societies must publish oertifled statements
twice a year of their assets and liabilities, directors must
keep properbooksof accounts,and the manager must verify
the balance-sheet by a statutory declaration. Power is given
to appoint special auditors. Companies are prohibited
from lending money on their own shares, and advances
made to directors must be filed with the Registrar-
General. It is made a penal offence for a company to
publish a misleading statement of the amount of its
capital. Dividends must only be paid out of profits, and
if this is contravened the directors are to be personally
liable for the amount illegally distributed. They are also
prevented from making a first or any issue of shares at a
premium until the company has been established at
least 12 months. No new companies are to be
permitted to use the words “savings,” “savings
bank,” “bank,” “banking company,” “banking
house,” “banking association,” or “banking institu-
tion." Increased restrictions are imposed on mort-
gages or assignments on the uncalled capital of a
company. The Registrar-General is to be informed where
branch registers are kept, and the shares must be dis-
tinguished from the principal register. Foreign com-
panies must register the names of their agents and situa-
tion of office. Power to alter the objects, form or con-
stitution of a company is subject to confirmation by the
court, which may also order, on a resolution of the share-
holders, capital to be reduced. The promoters or
directors of a company are held responsible for an untrue
prospectus under which it is formed, whilst it is a mis-
demeanor to make a false declaration with a view of in.
dueing persons to take shares. Promoters are also liable ■


to pay compensation to the person or persons imsle d
by a false prospectus. In connection with the winding-
up of companies directors are prohibited from becoming
liquidators, and if it is proved that any promoter, director,
manager, liquidator, or auditor has misapplied the money
or property of a company, the court may assess damages
against them. The powers of liquidators are strictly
defined, and the almost impregnable position they at pre-
sent occupy is removed by bringing them more under the
control of the law.
These provisions make it certain that—provid-
ing the bill gets through—there will be no
more alleged “banks” in Victoria with £lOO
or so paid-up capital, or with a paid-up capital
which consists mostly of the p.n’s of the
promoters, who are also the managers and
hold their own “securities” for safe cus-
tody. Also, it will make it certain that
the shareholders won’t he all men of straw,
and will prevent company - mongers dazz-
ling the public with that bogus security
■which comes of the announcement that the
.capital is £4,000,000, of which £500,000 is
subscribed and £25,000 paid up. Still, despite
the many good points in the measure, there
seems to be something wanting. Directors are
to be liable for dividends paid out of capital,
. a nd for money lost through misleading prospec-
tuses, and the books are to be properly kept,
; and the balance-sheet officially verified, and a
large number of other things of much value are
to be done. But when they are left undone,
;aud the directors are hauled before the courts
; as a consequence, the Bench will probably
.decide, as usual, that they were not left undone
within the meaning of the Act.” And where
(the Act says that directors and promoters are
tto be responsible for dividends wrongfully paid,
.or for money invested on the strength of
misleading prospectuses, the law will doubt-
less decide that this means they are respon-
sible if they had a criminal knowledge of the
wrongfulness of their own actions at the time
they were committed; and as no man can abso-
lutely prove what another man knew or didn’t
know, the same old snag will get in the way
again. Considering that every influential Vic.
bank-wrecker up to date was held by the law to
have no guilty knowledge of anything, under
any circumstances whatever, this difficulty is
serious. The shortest way out of it would be
to enact that directors, promoters and managers
shall be assumed to know what they are doing,
like other people, and that when one of them,
while in his sane mind, commits an illegal act
he shall be criminally responsible, whether lie
.knew he was committing it or not. This
proviso wouldn’t be nearly so brutal as it sounds.
‘When a man picks another’s pocket in the
.street, the law always assumes that he knew he
,was doing it. And when a man does the same
jin a bank, the law could assume the same thing
vwithout any injury to its sense of justice.
Prisons and Criminals.
lEditor Bulletin, —Your scheme for the reor-
ganisation of our prison system would, if intro-
duced under present conditions, work splendidly
jin theory, but in practice would be a dead
Ifailure. Whv not go to the root of the evil and
make a code of regulations and general orders
uinder which gaol-warders can discharge their
duties with humanity to prisoners and benefit
to the community ? It is not the system that
is altogether at fault, but the harsh treatment
imposed on warders by the regulations, which, in
many instances, make them irritable and unfit to
properly maintain good discipline. Let me give
two instances of many. First, warders (I speak
of New South Wales gaols) perform long
hours of constant duties, from 12 to 14 a day,
which is sufficient to sour the temper of any
man and make him indifferent as to his sur-
roundings. Should a warder be absent on
account of sickness, he must pay a substitute
ffis. fid. per diem to do his duty during his ab-
sence. This is justice with a vengeance, con-
sidering that a 3rd-class warder, who receives
;Bs. per day, most likely has to support himself
.and family on the balance—ls. fid. Second,
.should a warder need a holiday to recoup his
(health, he must pay a substitute and all his
.expenses to and from his destination ! In fact,
(those men are the worst-treated of all Govern-
iment employes, and yet we don’t hear the self-
sstyled democratic Labor-party ever raise its
woice in protest. Let gaol officials be treated
like men. The imprisoned should be governed
by the free, not by men who themselves are
practically imprisoned. —J ustice.
The N.S.W. Lead-Poisoning Regulations.
When Parliament essays the task of protecting
labor against the unscrupulous or careless
employer, there should be an exact understand-
ing as to the measure of protection wanted.
When that is settled, the resultant act or regu-
lations should crystallise the needed safeguards
in terse, unmistakeable English. At Broken
Hill the mineowners, it was complained, took
insufficient precautions for the prevention of
lead-poisoning; therefore, the local member
piloted through the Assembly an amendment to
the Mining Act authorising the issue of regula-
tions, which, when subsequently issued, were
based on the recommendations of the Lead
(Commission. Said regulations are an eminent
.example of what such a code ought not to be.
It has been found that the regulation which
practically prohibits the employment of boys
under 16 inflicts needless hardship in scores
of cases without commensurate benefit.
Although the drafters wisely made it illegal to
work in certain situations without a respirator,
they were silent as to who was to provide the
respirators, and the mining co. s have evidenced
a curious partiality for workmen who buy their
own. The clauses dealing with clothes-chang-
ing, the supply of drinking-water, &c., are
meant well, but are couched in pain-
fully deciepit English, which obscures their
meaning and probably renders them legally im-
potent. One clause describes several systems
of supplying drinking-water in lead-infected
neighborhoods. But the “ shall ” which would
make one at least of these imperative is varied
in places by the semi-optional “ should ;
the plentiful use of the conjunction “or in
the one long sentence into which the descrip-
tion and directions for application of the several
systems is massed, allows the mine-owners, if
they wish to be on the safe side and adopt one,
to take which they like. Finally, as if to
.cuoure that the water-supply clause shall
be a dead letter, it winds up “or the
miners may take into the mine tea
or water in cans,” &c. The trail of
the lame and halting “should” is, in fact,
all over the code. There is to be a bath and
changing-room caretaker, “ and it should be
part of his duty ” to do certain things. Then
there is a puzzle, not as to the meaning of
“ should,” but as to what the drafters intended
it should mean, which even an interpretation
clause could not settle. Every underground
workman “should” change his clothes before
descending to, and on coming from, work —one
regulation says. Another decrees that certain
other workmen “ shall be under the same obli-
gation to change before going on shift as under-
ground hands.” Yet there is no obligation in
the first instance—or if such was contemplated,
is the recalcitrant workman who neglects to
conform or the mining co. to be held liable for
breach of the regulations ? Revision is urgently
needed.
The Relics of the Star Chamber.
A Bulletin correspondent: Is it generally
known that the Privy Council is simply the
dregs of the Star Chamber, that it is all that
was left of that institution after the Long Par-
liament wiped it out as far as England was con-
cerned. An institution hateful to the English
freeman, obnoxious to English liberty, but good
enough for “ His Majesty’s colonies and planta-
tions!” The Judicial Committee of the P.C.
still preserves a good old Star Chamber tradi-
tion. Its judgments (by the way, not judg-
ments at all in form) are concocted in secret;
no one knows whether all the Judges have
agreed or whether there may not be two out of
five dissenting. This reduces immensely the
value of their decisions, for the opinions of
three nincompoops go out to the “colonial”
world as though they were the opinions of five men.
The 'personnel of the P.C. has been much im-
proved of late. But still it is far from perfect,
even from the purely legal point of view. The
English courts treat its decisions very cava-
lierly, even the puny Divisional Courts showing
no hesitation in over-riding them. The Council’s
imperfect acquaintance with Australian institu-
tions has resulted in bringing about an undesir-
able sense of insecurity in regard to the Torrens
system of land-transfer, which will become
worse when the system is adopted in England
and the weighty Court of Appeal begins to play
ducks and drakes with the P.C. dicta. The
absurdity of the system cannot be too often or
too strenuously pointed out. A batch of Judges
(the really able members of the batch deem it
infra dig. to be present) selected in a haphazard
fashion (till very lately), trained mostly on the
lines of English jurisprudence (the most insular,
bigoted, and narrow of all), sit as a final court
ofappeal to determine the subtlest questions of
law that can arise among nations of ages
varying from Noah’s to yesterday’s, some of
whom have lived under custom for centuries; and
adjudicate at the same sittings on questions of
English, French (old regime), Roman, Dutch,
Danish, Maltese, Spanish, Turkish, Maori,
and ancient Norwegian laws, to say no-
thing of Hindoo, Parsee, and Mahomme-
dan customs, and colonial admiralty appeals.
A more unwieldy and unsuitable contrivance
for dispensing justice to the most heterogeneous
peoples ever known in the history of the
world cannot be imagined. The Romans
early gave as good a supreme Judge to
their subject peoples as they had for them-
selves, and, as a result of the work of this Judge
(the praetor peregrinus), an almost perfect legal
system was evolved —which was respected by
the Romans themselves, and, in fact, afterwards
superseded their own. But our P.C. is not
respected by either Englishman or “colonial.”
A case in point is a recent decision on the law
of negligence, in which a Melbourne tailor and
his wife were, unfortunately for themselves, the
leading figures. The decision in this case is the
laughing-stock of all writers on negligence, and
the shuttlecock of every tinpot court in England
and Ireland. It is not even clear whether it
binds Australia, for an eminent English
Judge dismissed his consideration of it by
contemptuously remarking that it was only
binding on the Supreme Court of Victoria.
—H.D.

Tue Midland Railway Shindy.
The biggest legal fight and the biggest bill for
law-expenses which any Australasian Govern-
ment ever encountered will probably eventuate
in Maoriland re the famous Midland Railway.
The situation regarding that disastrous and
crooked scheme—drawn up by Stout and Vogel
in their frantic efforts to resuscitate the dead
boom —is altogether remarkable. The co. has
received, as bonus on the work already done,
land to the extent of several hundreds of thou-
sands of acres. It made four widely-severed
bits of railroad, which, unless completed, are of
little use to anybody. Then it stopped work, and
demanded cash instead of land, and, by the
stoppage, the land already granted was, to a
great extent, made a dead loss. The co.
threatened to drag the Govt, into a huge and
expensive litigation over its “ claims -or into
an equally expensive arbitration. I iually,
Govt, seized the line already built under the
terms of the contract, and now it is in an inte-
resting situation. There will be an enormous
amount of law over the matter. If Maoriland
loses, it will have to pay a massive bill of costs
and heavy damages, and then it will have
to go back to the old deadlock, and have
another big litigation, or arbitration, over the
co.’s “ claims.” If it loses there also, there will
be more costs, and another big bill for damages ;
if it wins, there will also be costs, and the co.
will probably remain on strike, and leave the
work in its present unfinished state, and after
all the sacrifices that have been made to get the
Midland line there will be no Midland line
after all. On the other hand, if the province
wins the first case, and thus asserts its right to
seize the unfinished railway, there will still be
costs to pay ; also, the nation must find the
money to finish the line if it is to be of any
value in opening up the country, and when it is
finished there is very little probability that it
will pay its way for the next 10 or 20 years.
There isn’t much to win if the couutiy does
win, and though the contract looks too explicit
to allow of any question, still the law is such
an anomalous kind of mule -Unit it -is just as
likely to lose. No legal document eau be made
so explicit that anyone can rely on it with
absolute confidence.
Dear Bulletin,— What is the matter with
“Government by the workingman for the woik-
ingman ?” Looking at the question from a
Socialist standpoint, I see no difference between
it “and Government by the people for the
people.” Latterday economists give to Labor
the natural produce of Labor, to every man
according as he produces. Surely this is ground
enough for the doctrine that the brain and hand
workers are really the people. The Labor-party,
as far as its name goes, is wide enough to include
everybody, from Jimmy Tyson to his station
cook, since all say, I suppose, that they
labor for their bread. There is nothing
in the constitution of the N.S.W. Labor
League to prevent a bank-director joining
it; it is not an association of manual
laborers —it is an association of workers. I admit
that the hob-nailed superstitionprevailsamongst
Labor-leaguers—as in S.A. It is a relic of the
time when a prominent plank of the Labor -
party’s platform was that it should not wear a
pot hat. But it is fast disappearing ; and the
most ornamental member of the present New
South Wales Parliament is a prominent and
trusted Labor man. Anyhow, the idle class are
responsible for the superstition. While they
consider it ungentlemanly to soil their hands
with honest toil, or to speak to a shabby
acquaintance in the street, so long will the virile
democracy of Australia thank God it was not
born a gentleman. — Railway Man.
It is alleged by their enemies that certain
Sydney solicitors have touts among the gaol
warders, who are employed, upon commission,
to persuade those prisoners entitled to money
from the Grown for their defence, to employ
the attorneys whom they represent. But this,
even if true, is no valid reason for the Crown s
pleading lack of funds, postponing the prepara-
tion of a capitally-arraigned man’s defence
until the first day of sittings, and then handing
the prisoner’s neck over to the care of an un-
paid sucking barrister, who, as Justice
Innes put it the other morning, needn’t attend
unless he chooses ! To Mr. Justice Simpson (in
the days of his Attorney-Generalship) is due
the credit of having first provided funds for
the defence of capitally-arraigned moneyless
prisoners. The system plainly has the approval
of his no less humane judicial colleagues, and
The Bulletin hopes that Attorney-General
Want will not bring about a rever-
sion to the old and cruel system. And
why should not their Honors emphatically
refuse to try any undefended prisoner capi-
tally arraigned ? Who would dare to blame
the Judge who, under such circumstances,
declined to hear the Crown Prosecutor ’ Upon
such matters the Judges should speak out loud.
Here is the Crown—which actually pays for
carriages and cabs to convey the members of
tlie N.S.W. Bar to vice-regal levees and spends,
say, £13,000 in erecting a statue to gaoler-
Governor Phillip '.—pleading “ N.S.F.” to
applications for the money necessary to defend
moneyless citizens on trial for their lives !
The fiscal attitude of the New South Wales
Labor-party is incomprehensible. The party
demands that the local worker should be com-
pelled bv statute to work only eight hours per
day, and that a respectable minimum wage
should be fixed. Then it demands that
he should compete on even terms with men
who work 10 or 12 or 14 hours a day for less
which is not commendable, but only
illogical and foolish. ihe short houis and
the minimum wage are Protection for the
foreign competitor; every artificial restric-
tion = placed by legal enactment upon an
industrial rival”is Protection, and this only
differs from the ordinary kind because it is
protecting the other man against us, instead of
protecting us against the other man. And if
the New SSuth Wales worker doesn’t protect
himself against the foreigner, as well as protect
the foreigner against him, then he is obviously
handicapped out of the race in every industry
that is open to foreign competition. The party
that thinks it can have higher wages and
shorter hours than anybody else, and yet com-
pete with everybody on equal terms, is expect-
ing a great deal.
Francis Abigail (just released from prison) and
Dr. Violette (surgeon to the gaol at Parra-
matta) affirm that N.S.W. treatment of juvenile
offenders simply breeds criminals by starving
them into incapacity to earn an honest livmg.
This they have seen with their own eyes. The
Comptroller of Prisons (who is the chief sup-
porter of the GouLD-gag) denies the charge. As
his denial must be based chiefly on the reports
of his own officials, it is worth little. It is
worth less still when one considers how fond
gaol-officialdom is of considering its own con-
venience the measure of right; how it is
tethered by red-tape ; how apt it is to regard
prisoners as blocks of wood, to be knocked into
shape with axe and mallet; how warmly it re-
sents outside interference with its methods.
The Bulletin is quite satisfied that Ur. Vio-
letta and Abigail speak the truth. The
N.S.W. gaol-system must be modelled by the
men at its head. These have been shown to
have neither heart nor brains ; and their system
must be like unto them.
Jos. Symes got a “not guilty ” verdict in the
criminal libel-action brought against him, but
has been imprisoned for 14 days by Chief
Justice Madden for contempt of court. That
is a trifle ; gaol is a fine place for reflection, and
the Liberator (how in its 11th year) will get
on all right for a fortnight. The tall, lank
Joseph— “ a man of sorrows ” if ever there was
oue _ u tters a hope that he has now seen the
end of his troubles. The Bulletin sincerely
hopes he hasn’t, for Australia can’t spare the
combative old atheist, who is, in his way,
almost as obstinate as the bigots he
attacks, but whose career is such a
monument of industry and talent used
with single-purposed sincerity that one glows
in contemplating it. For some 20 years
now, in season and out of season, with good
taste, bad taste, and no taste aLall, Jus. Bvme.
has been doing every day of Ids life three men *
work with tongue and pen to spread the truth
as he knows it, the light as he sees it. He ha-
fought continuously against calumny and mis
representation; the defection of friends and the
attacks of ~open and secret enemies ; private
affliction and public scorn ; frowns, pity,
and laughter. He has been poorer than.
Job all the time, his Liberator is hardly
ever out of the valley of the shadow of
death, and just now he badly wants £5O to
pay rent and taxes and give him a fresh start.
Such a man can only reach the end of his
troubles in two ways. One is to stop struggling
and float with the conventional stream. lie
other way is that painted in the magnificent
close of the magnificent “ Beauchamp s Career,
when the great Lord Romfrey stands thunder-
ing on the beach to direct the search for the
bodv of the drowned Radical, whose life has
been so pathetically fruitless, so grandly unsuc-
cessful. Perhaps the end of Jos. Bymes troubles
may come when a long, loose-jointed corpse with
grizzled hair and furrowed face is found floating
on the peaceful bosom of the Yarra, to receive
as guerdon and epitaph the title which
Heine chose for himself — “A Soldier in the
Liberation-War of Humanity.”
The assertion that the present low price of
candles in New South Wales is owing to the tall
in tallow, and not to Protection and the conse-
quent increase of local supplies, is knocked end-
ways, or partially so, by the following tact.
During ’B4 and ’B5 the price of tallow in Europe
fell from £37 to £24 per ton, but the importers
ring which bossed the Sydney market kept the
price steadily at 9d. per lb. Ihen, in 86, the
protected local industry started and prices
straightway went down with a thud to b.jd.
They have since been further reduced. From
this it appears that reductions in the price of
raw material don’t necessarily mean a reduction
to the consumer so long as the importing ting
has control of the market; also, that Protection
does make t hings cheaper —sometimes. Yet if the
duty is taken off the importer can, and probably
will, sell at 5d., or even less, per lb., until he
has extinguished the local maker, when he will
shove the prices up to the old level once more,
and during the fivepenny period there will be
much joy over the cheapness which comes
through Freetrade. The gist of the thing is
that the imported Freetrade article would
generally be cheaper than the local protected
one if the importer would sell as cheaply as he
can—which he won’t, except under compulsion,
and never will do unless his “ ring is burst up
by local competition. And the whole 4 reetra.de
doctrine of cheapness is built on the assumption
that the importer does sell at the lowest pos-
sible rate—which he doesn’t; and that he
doesn’t form rings to keep up prices—which he
does ; and that it is only needful to show that
goods can be lauded in Australia more cheaply
than they can be locally produced, quite ignor-
ing the fact that they never are landed at that
price, or anything like it, until the pressure of
local competition begins.
The Tichborne story told by Walter Lee, of
Glebe, seems to throw a suspicion of light upon
the origin of Orton’s imposture, and Jean
Ldie’s incidental perjury. Walter Lee gives
us an Osprey schooner as an actual fact. He
sailed on it from Adelaide to Melbourne in
July, 1854; believes that a young gentleman
passenger was Roger Tichborne, and that
one of the crew, called Arthur, was Orton.
The young gentleman landed at Geelong, went
on a prolonged spree, and was reported to have
died. Jean Luie was never aboard the Osprey.
The merit of Lee’s yarn is its moderation he
doesn’t protest too much. It sounds like a
probable explanation of many things difficult
to account for in the Tichborne trial evidence.
If Roger took to Geelong whisky and died, or
was murdered, there is ground for supposing
that he may never have written home. If
Orton knew of Roger’s life and death at Gee-
long he had at least one dead-bird in hand
when he started to shoot for the Tichborne
stakes. Also, if Orton and Castro sailed on
the Osprey from Adelaide, where Walter Lee
joined the ship (always assuming that Lee
hasn’t persuaded himself to imagine the exist-
ence of the craft aforesaid), it is possible
that Orton was one of the crew of the
Osprey at Valparaiso, or some other
port, where Roger, escaped from the
wreck of the Bella, came aboard. Which
would explain the apparent honesty of Jean
Luie’s evidence in days of yore. Orton had
loaded him up with facts, not fiction, and Luie.
was merely lying as to bis own personal con-
nection with the Osprey. Lee’s suggestive
reminiscences of that much-doubted schooner
are as well worth acceptance as the Claimant’s re-
cent “ confession.” The wily Fatman has doubt-
less confessed frankly enough everything that
happened from the time when Lady Tichborne’s
advt. came to his ears, but he may have denied
all knowledge of Roger in the fifesh for reasons
often hinted at during the famous trial and
since. The editor of the paper that published
Orton’s confession is inclined to credit Orton
with a knowledge of Roger Tichborne. He
has cross-examined his man, and found the con-
fession rather shaky on its legs, maybe.

“ La Cigale ” is doing one week of im-
mense business at the Lyceum, by way of winding
up the operatic season. There have been more
operas crowded into the present season than were
ever put into so short a space of time before, by
way of showing Nellie Stewart in as many differ-
ent aspects as possible before that favorite starts
in the track of the setting sun for the foggy land
of Bull. Next Friday will be the very last night
of Nellie before she quits this bereaved continent,
and, by way of leaving the brightest possible im-
pression behind her, she is playing the rather un-
satisfactory part of Marton with even more than
her usual brilliance, and “La Cigale’ is doing a
much wilder boom than its own rather slender
merits deserve. Miss Nellie was announced to
take a benefit in “ Ma Mie Rosette” on Wednes-
day, and with true benevolence the gross proceeds
are to be handed over to the poor of Sydney, so
it Is Nellie’s benefit in name only. In recognition
of which kindness The Bulletin hereby gives
three cheers, and would give more if its speaking-
voice hadn’t been affected by the weather.
* m * *
The alleged Gaiety Co., after a brilliant
dazzle in Melbourne, which has reduced the
scanty remains of the local johnnie-dom to imbe-
cility, opens at the Lyceum on Saturday with
“A Gaiety Girl.’’ The rush which ensued when
this was known nearly swept “the Finn ” off its
four feet, and the circumstances were so desperate
that it was found necessary to sell the seats for
the first performance by auction. If the plan had
been opened at Nicholson’s in the usual way a
large number of persons would assuredly have
been killed in the rush, and Nicholson’s stock
would have been trodden underfoot and utterly
wrecked ; and when the dead were removed out
of the ruins W. and M. would have been tried
before Judge Windeyer, and convicted late on a
Saturday night under the capacious hanging laws
of N.S. Wales.
# * * *
Bartley Campbell’s Californian drama,
“My Partner,” ends at Her Majesty’s to-night
(Thursday), after a run of only five nights. This
unfortun ite suddenness was forced on the manage-
ment by previous arrangements, which is to be
regretted, for Bartley Campell’s hero is a manly,
upstanding individual, with whom every person
possessed of anything like a soul must feel in
sympathy, and, though Mr. Rignold is struggling
with an aggravated influenza, he makes the charac-
ter deeply impressive. The funny man is a China-
man, and, as a Chinaman has no dignity to lose,
he doesn’t look such a melancholy spectacle as
anybody else would under like circumstances.
Moreover, Tolano makes an artistic Chow, and,
when he bowls out the hard-faced iniquity of the
story, he does it on logical grounds, which the
funnyman seldom does. Besides, his pathetic
devotion to the distressed girl with the blind
staggers, and the infant that she didn’t ought to
have, is a pleasant feature in his character. The
bad man, who is responsible for the infant, has
many redeeming traits about him, and, the audi-
ence feels, with a glow under its waistcoat, that he
would have put things right if the much-worse
man hadn’t prematurely dug a knife into his spine.
The play is ingeniously constructed, and G. Rig-
nold lies so magnificently to get the girl with the
infant out of a tight place, and so joyously hangs
the man who possesses her miserable secret and
wants to give it away, and shuts down with such
vigor on the Roman parent when he attempts to
make the oration which the disgusting heavy
father always makes to his daughter under such
circumstances, that the audience feels good
towards him all round. The prodigal daughter
has come home in so many dramas, and been
thrown out into the rain by her corrugated parent,
that it is a glorious thing to see that parent shut
hard down upon just as he is beginning his weary
old harangue about the disgrace which has come
upon his escutcheon, or his coat-of-arms, or his
clothes-trunk, or something.
* * * *
Miss Amy Roselle and Mr. Arthur Dacre
open at Her Majesty’s on Friday, in “ Esther
Sandraz ” —a powerful play, new to Sydney audi-
ences—and “ A RpgularFix.” The Bulletin has
already had occasion to speak in high terms of these
two artistes, and they ought to fill even G.R.’s capa-
cious theatre. Miss Roselle proved in Mel-
bourne that her great English reputation was
fully justified, and Dacre is a versatile actor,
who covers a deal of ground in more ways than
one. The pair have brought with them a number
of plays new to Australia, including “ A Wife’s
Ordeal,” by G. R. Sims, “A Bunch of Violets,”
Sardou’s “ Scrap of Paper,” and the above-men-
tioned “ Esther Sandraz.” There should be a big
house at G. Rignold’s theatre on Friday night.

Miss Maggie Moore continues doing her
“last weeks” at Sydney Royal, and “Sweet
Innisfail ” is keeping up well—partly by reason of
Oily Deoring’s process-server, for whom this paper
has conceived a lasting affection as the most
capable and original process-server it knows.
This person’s shape, which is altogether unpre-
oedented, seems to he arrived at by wearing a
brick in his pants, and an unabridged dictionary
on his back, and a large knob of coal inside his
vest. Anyhow, a strong desire is growing up to
have him surgically inspected, on the ground that
he looksjike a camel which has been mashed by a
falling gum-tree. •*>.

The Empire (formerly the Opera House)
fills considerably of late, and seems likely to take
root in the great soul of the public. There are
two or three attractions at this house. Banvard’s
“Leap from the Dome” is a thrilling episode
which makes the onlooker feel as if the door-key
had gone down his back, and the trapeze ant is a
bone-shaking performance. Miss Ada Colley’s
singing is something worth listening to and think-
ing seriously about afterwards, and some of the
blushful maidens of the 00. are very fair to look
upon.

At the auction of seats for the first night
of the Gaiety Cos., at Sydney Lyceum, Mr.
Barney Allen, the metallician, bought the first
box submitted for’ten guineas.. Seats in the d.c.
realised as high as 245., and good positions
averaged, probably, 11s. Most of the front seats,
where a full view of the co.’s beautiful legs could
be obtained, were purchased “regardless.” But
the function was not what it should have been.
The auctioneer, Mr. Henry Gorman, as befitted his
early career as a Methodist Church office-bearer,
left all the color and poetry and dazzle and frill
and legs out of jus discourse. The “ Firm ” ought
to have hired* Joe Tolano for the occasion at a
high salary, and furnished him with an auc-
tioneer’s licence, and let him discourse at large
about the Gaiety girls, and the understandings
thereof, and cheer on Moses and Abraham and
Isaac and Jacob to destruction by bursts of poetry.
An auctioneer of the solemn, corner-allotment
variety is utterly lost under such circumstances.
* * » «
Susan’s Rebellion was promptly put down
at Melbourne Princess’s, and on Saturday the
8.8. management staged “An Ideal Husband,”
-by “the author of Lady Windermere’s Fan.”
Everybody knows that the author in question is a
calamitous person named Oscar Wilde. The
brain responsible for “ A_n Ideal Husband ” is
shockingly diseased; the hand which penned the
manuscript is reported to be picking oakum.
Therefore, Oscar Wilde’s, play was placed before
the public as a convicted felon, so to speak. They
saw it under the disadvantages of gaol-clothes and
absurdly abbreviated hair, they scanned its fea-
tures in eager search for signs of turpitude, and
yet felt hound to confess that this was one of the
most pleasing plays'they have gazed upon.
In Sydney, where “ An Ideal Husband ” appeared
behind the spikes at the end of the season,
The Bulletin was struck by the prisoner’s
healthy aspect. The sentiment of “Lady Win-
dermere’s Fan” seemed to pride itself on being
contemptible, hut “An Ideal Husband ” bids for
respect.as well as cynical admiration. The slender
plot is very moral. A husband, who has been
guilty of only one financial sin against his high
principles, is made to suffer much at the hands of
a wicked adventuress and an abnormally good
wife. The wife learns to temper her rigid virtue
with a little ordinary feminine forgiveness* and
the friend of the family is a well-meaning indi-
vidual, given to talking sense when he isn’t
spouting sham paradoxes. Also, the father of
this friend reprimands him for his reckless habit
of spouting as aforesaid, and the wicked adven-
turess, who is the most inveterately “smart”
talker of them all, and who even thinks epigrams
aloud when she is left by herself in a room, gets
clean knocked out in the third act. The fourth
act is devoted to high principle, cheerful love-
making, willing self-sacrifices, connubial devotion,
and all that is most desirable in this world. Oscar
Wilde got this clever work from the sound side of
his intellect, whereas “ Lady Windermere’s Fan ”
was written, by the debased oakum-picker. The
two plays ’'should not be linked together on the
bill. Better to ascribe “ An Ideal Husband ” to
O. Wilde, deceased.
# • •
The cast of characters is the same as in
Sydney. Miss Olliffe’s picturesque adventuress
introduces the new actress to a Melbourne
audience, and the audience considers her a fine,
imposing, sweet-voiced artist, with a charming
little foot and ample ability to play her part.
Miss Faber, as the wife, is another special success,
whilst Messrs. Titheradge, Ward, Boucicault, and
Miss Noble are less special, but none the less
valuable in their places. “ An Ideal Husband ”
deserves a great success.

At Melb. Royal the two superior villain 8
are working out their respective destructions to
flourishing business, and the noble Baker is still
chipping in a casual reference to The Union Jack
just for the sake of appearances. Otherwise this
popular melodrama would have no visible claim
to its title. Its purpose as a military and naval
spectacle is not to draw attention to Hing-
land’s glory, but to expose the awful
character of a captain and an army con-
tractor. It even suggests that the British
bayonets would double up if suddenly brought
into contact with foreign stummicks. “ The
Fatal Card” succeeds “The Union Jack’’ on
Saturday. Bland Holt is making so much money
that lie can afford to withdraw a lucrative specula-
tion for fun, or perhaps his season is limited to a
certain number of weeks, and he has sworn to pro-
duce as many novelties as possible within the time.
Anyhow, “ The Fatal Card” will he played on
Saturday.

The death of a young wife is, by time-
honored custom, treated as a cause for unwhole-
some lamentation in newspaper obituary and
funeral notices. If the deceased lady was an act-
ress, the wail of the flabby-minded journalist be-
comes positively offensive. It is then a rouged,
powdered, spangled, lime-lit wail, the cry of an
amateur mummer in a burst of artificial distress—
an insult to common sense. Melbourne Argus
sobbed theatrically over the burial of Mrs. Tapley.
“ They lowered the little coffin down into the
earth, and a glorious sunset spread its rosy hues
over |he faces of all as the last words were said.
Then' the curtain of the night fell slowly, and the
little actress went home to rest.” “The extreme
youth of the dead actress, the pathetic circum-
stances of her death, and the presence of her
bereaved husband, who had doffed the mot-
ley in Sydney on Friday last only to put on
the garb of mourning here on Monday, lent an
infinite pathos to the last scene.” Maudlin drivel
and stagey cant. There is no exceptional pathos
in an actor’s bereavement. Everybody wears
motley in his daily business. And what of this
precious paragraph : “It was just in front of the
Princess’s Theatre that a pathetic little incident
occurred. As the carriage containing the wreaths
passed along a large white lily became detached
from one of the tributes and fell in the dust. In
a second a young girl with tearful eyes stepped
out from the crowd, rescued the flower from
under the feet of the horses, and pressed it closely
to her bosom.” Ugh !

tised fable. She is at the head of her profession here,

Poor little Vi. Varley was an unassuming, unpretentious,
girl off the stage. She used to go about with the im-
pression that she hadn’t a grain of ability, and it was,,
only the constant pressure of relatives and admirers.,
which kept her up to sticking-point. After continuous-,
playing with the Comic Opera Co. for eight years, she .
quarrelled with “the Firm” some months ago, and,
severed her connection finally with that organisation. A a
good many efforts have been made since to get her to ,
appear again, but her dispute with W. and M. marked a ,
resolve to quit the Australian stage until she had con-
quered London. Her mind w’as full of the. plans for a ,
course of study under Randegger, and she was bent on ,
making a big effort to outshine a certain other Australian ,
star who somehow didn’t manage to scintillate over-
brilliantly in the London firmament.

The fashionable criminal charge of the
present season in N.S. Wales is that form of
assault upon a female which involves the services
of the sheriff’s deputy’s final deputy. In the
majority of instances, the prosecutrixes have repen-
ted of their first assertions and admitted the verity
of certain impeachments, or the accusations have
been plainly disproved. But as so many false
starts have been publicly made, it would seem as
though Cornstalkian maledom is at present largely
under the influence of blackinaildom.
Richard Chaffey Baker, M.A., now Presi-
dent of S. A. Legislative Council, is the strongest
Conservative in local politics. He it was whom
Premier Kingston was so anxious to slay in a duel
three years ago. Although still comparatively
young, it is more than a quarter of a century
since Mr. Baker was first sent to the Assembly for
Barossa, and his term of continuous service in the
Council has been longer than that of any of his
present colleagues. For an aggregate space of 784
days he has held office in various Ministries, and ,
it is now 18 months since he attained to the Presi-
dent’s chair in the House, which for half-a-dozen
years previously had been practically under his ;
thumb. He was beginning to think that Queen ,
Victoria had forgotten him, and if he had been,
passed over again on the ’present occasion he
might have drifted into deep and desperate dis-,
loyalty. Though they speak when they meet,
officially, there is still a great gulf between King-
ston and Baker, and last year the former was very
markedly left out from the list of invitations fcV»
the President’s dinner.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Bulletin has long maintained that all
Australian borrowings should be done locally, and that
the local market would supply money as cheaply as it
could be obtained in London. The result of the South
Australian loan, floated last week, proves this contention
to be correct. Compared with the results of three other
loans recently issued in London, matters stand somewhat
as follows :-—M loriland, which has floated no loans for a
good many years and has now the best credit of all the
provinces,'got its money in London at £3 6s. 6d. per cent,
interest. Westralia raised its money in the same city at
i'3 Bs. per cent., and the Queensland loan costs about
£3 11s. per cent. This is calculated on the amount
received, after deducting accrued interest and probable
expenses. The S. A. loan was floated in Adelaide
at £3 10s., more than twice the amount required
being offered at very short notice. The loan was
issued at a fixed price ; under the tender system it
would probably have realised more and thus re-
duced the interest. Moreove*, Adelaide is not a
financial centre like Melbourne and Sydney, in either
of which cities the operation would probably have
been conducted still more successfully. Yet, after allow-
ing for the saving in expenses, the S.A. Government has
do'ne decidedly better in Adelaide than the Queenslanders
did in London, and has done very nearly as well as
Maoriland and Westralia. And, in any case, it is far
cheaper in the end to pay £3 10s. per cent, interest to
oneself than £3 6s. 6d. per cent, to anybody else. That
is just where the difference between local and foreign
borrowing oomes in.
* * • •
The Colonial Bank of Australasia meets its
creditors on the 17th with a proposal to reduce interest
on deposits to 24 per cent, for five years, and thereafter
to pay current rates, whatever these may be. One-fifth
of the 5, 6 and 7-year deposits are to be paid on maturity
and the other four fifths extended for five years. The 10-
year deposits are to be extended for three years. The
one excellent feature about the Colonial management was
that it never took British deposits. Its creditors being
almost all in Victoria, it will probably get its arrangement
through, while the City of Melb., which started much
earlier, is still struggling to explain things to indignant
Scotch creditors who have a vague idea that their money
has been stolen by aboriginals. The Colonial, it is also
understood, intends writing off a good deal of its capital
as lost when a new Act is passed making such a proceed-
ing possible. * » *
The leading bank-managers of Australia have
held a conference and drafted the outline of anew bank-
ing bill, which the various Parliaments are to be invited
to accept. It provides for a stringent definition of what
constitutes a bank, for certain modifications in the quar-
terly returns, for putting the note issue on anew basis,
and for a more definite form of audit. The scheme is
not yet made public, but it is safe to assume that it deals
with all these matters from a banker’s standpoint much
more than from the standpoint of the public. Meanwhile,
the present N.S.W. Bank Notes Act is near its expira-
tion ; that comprehensive Banking Act which was
promised is still missing ; and Premier Reid’s State Bank
proposals are very remote and intangible. Under these
circumstances, and until the State Bank oomes along,
some legislation is evidently needed to fill the gap. One
effective way to with the note difficulty would be to
provide that any existing bank may issue notes to any
xtent, on the one condition that it deposits with
the Treasury, Government stock to the full amount of
its issue, said stock to be held as security for the notes
and used to redeem them in case of default. On
the deposit being made the Treasury would issue
a licence to print and circulate nobs to a
certain definite amount. The issue being thus
practically guaranteed by the State the present regu-
lation, by which paper-money is redeemable in gold
at the bank’s head office only, would injure nobody.
Most banks would probably deposit a much larger amount
of stock than was required to cover their ordinary issue,
and with this security on hand the Government could
tide over any sudden crisis by declaring the notes so
covered to be.legal tender for any period that might be
necessary. * * »
