Seventeen years ago Christchurch (M.L.) Drain-
r Board reported joyfully that it had borrowed in
London £200,000 at 6 per cent, fixed fqy 50 years, so that
the loan can’t be repaid or the interest reduced -until
1928. Thai was in boom-time. Now the decrease in pro-
perty values has made the 6 per cent, an awful and in-
tolerable burden, and, judging from present appearances,
Christchurch may, in a few years, go broke after the
fast don of Oamaru and New Plymouth. In addition to
£500,000 advanced by the State, Maoriland local bodies,
of one kind and another, have borrowed locally £1,100,000,
and in London over £5,000,000. Of this amount nearly
one-half is at rates varying from 6to 9 per cent!_ The
British Shylock has been worse than the local one in his
dealings, more than half his money being lent at six and
s ven per cent. Under these circumstances, the going
broke of various Maoriland local bodies can be viewed
with complacency. The probable end of the business will
he that Govt, will offer the various creditors 3 or 3* per
cent, with a State guarantee, and put a strict limit to
future borrowing, or, better still, shut down on all bor-
r wing except from the Treasury ; and put in some kind
of a receiver to collect the interest and sinking-fund till
the liability is cleared of. This process would save the
country fully £120,000 per annurn-avery large amount
for so small a community,

The Australian-butter market, writes The Bul-
letin's London correspondent by last mail, is completely
demoralised owing to a very large lot h iving been thrown
on (he market-at low prices by a bank which had ad-
vanced upon it. Some was sold down to 265. per owt.
This should prove a lesson to shippers, for far more is
being sent over just now than the market can comfort-
ably absorb. The brokers and dealers are in despair, and
are wondering where the thing is going to end. Why does
not somebody try to discover some other means of pre-
serving butter than Uy freezing, for this process most
Undoubtedly bas a deleterious effect upon the butter,
especially when it is kept long.

• • * *
The North Sydney Investment and Tramway
Co.—now burst—was formed to acquire 1000 acres of
land at North Shore. The co. bought the land for
£512,000. The man who sold it to the co. and thus left
that concern to hold the huge and dropsical infant, seems
to have paid £500,000 for it a few weeks earlier. The
man he goUit from had bought it a month previously for
£300,000 from one Billyard, and Billyard had acquired
it a few days earlier for £200,000. If the rise was in any
sense a natural one, then it was a most remarkable rise ;
and if the land rose 150 per cent, in a few months simply
because there was a chance to sell out ultimately to a
fool of a company then it was—well, what was it?
* « • •
Some years ago a 00. was formed in a western
N.S.W. town to build a malt-house and begin malting.
The project fell through because the oidy bripkmaker in
the district then able to produce the required number
and quality of bricks was a Good Templar, high in the
order, who considered that the manufacture of bricks for
a malt-house would be a violation of his pledge 1
* • * «
The exodus of population has ceased from the
Melb. boom suburbs—mainly for lack of population— but
the exodus of houses is in full swing. Whole terraces of
wooden erections have been carted away, and daily one
sees distorted cottages, threatening to fall to pieces every
minute, being carried through Melb. streets to more
favorable letting localities. The man who invents a safe
and cheap transport for brick houses would benefit many
landlords. And if they could only start shipping brick
houses to the other provinces?
* * • •
Re pawnshops. In England a pawnbroker’s
rates are Jd. per month for every 25., or sd. in the £,
The pledge must be kept 12 months without selling. In
Brisbane the charge is 2s. in the £ per month, or over 120
per cent, per annum, pledge being sold at the end of three
months. Here's an opening for the Labor members in
the way of restrictive legislation. How about a State
pawnbroking department ?

The Kaffir boom has set everybody wild, and
the most ridiculous prices are being paid for shares,
writes Tub Bulletin's London ' correspondent. _ Take
Robinson’s, for instance, the original capital of which is, I
believe, £2,750,000. The market is valuing it at nearly
£25 000,000, that is, £9 for the £1 share ! Other mines
are selling in proportion. Had the Londonderry not
knocked the bottom out of VVestrali.ans, some of the rub-
bish in that part of the world would also be at ridiculous
prices, hut, unfortunately for promoters who are loaded
up with scrip, prices .have moved the wrong way when
they have moved at all.
* ♦ » *
Central (8.H.) directors agree to the postpone-
ment of the “confirmatory” meeting, and from present
appearances the sale to the "American” syndicate will
not he made. Had the directors resisted the postpone.-
ment proposal nasty things would have been said atymt
the non-reporting of the kaolin “ find ” at the first meet-
ing. This much may at least be said in their favor, that
their general manager, Randolph Adams, is not likely to
have given them any sensational report upon the striking
of the body, or for the first two or three days. He has
the reputation of persistently under-estimating—on the
whole, an excellent failing, but, as this case proves,
awkward at times.

« ♦ ♦ . *
At one time it was unsafe for reporter or visitor
to speak on the B.H. Proprietary mine of a “ creep.’
There was no such word in Mr. Howell s vocabulary ;
though in any case, Mr. HoweH was not responsible for
the creeps that did occur. But others reign now, where-
fore some men on the mine appear to try who can talk
loudest about creeps. Worst of all, this practice, an
obviously mean one, isn’t repressed. The same thing
can be detected in the newspaper reports. Formerly
these movements were pooh-poohed ; now they are sub-
sidences and creeps, and v'ery serious things at that.

Reported that offers have been made for Junc-

* • • •
Junction North has started work again. Little
more will probably be heard of the garnet sandstone
which boomed the mine two years ago. Sh ireholders are
sick of the name of garnet sandstone. Operations will he
directed to the development of the sulphides, of which
exjbensiv e bodies are known to exist in the mine, and
which, like those of the adjacent Junction, are generally
understood to be of a special class. Some part of the
leaching plant, erected originally to treat the garnet
sandstone—the crushing machinery, for instance —may be
used for sulphide dressing.
• ♦ • •
Mining co. directors have learned something
from the banking people. Block 10 uses its convenient
sulphide dump to come and go on ; and now another B.H.
mine uses its stores to a like effect.

Salman, one of America’s greatest constructive

and recommends the co. to treat ail its ore at Port
Pirie.
* • * •
The evolution of the Cobar copper-mine is an
object-lesson in N.S.W. mining. For years it was worked
profitably for copper alone, no allowance being made for
its gnld-eontenis, in fact, it is hut a few years since the
co. was made aware of the gold —by Fattier Curran, it is
said. About 18 months ago it was taken on tribute, by
men who had no previous experience in copper-smeinng
beyond that got by reading Dr. Peters’ work. Per aid of
the gold they make a fair profit, and, with a low copper-
market, the co. have paid a,div. out of the" royally. It
would he interesting to estimate the gold lost by the co.,
and made by copper-buyers in England, when the mines
rich oxidised ores were being treated years ago. -

The weak part of the Broken Hill sulphide-pro-
cess patented bv Ashcroft is the cost of recovering the
zinc by electricity. The motive-power required to treat
large quantities is enormous, and every cwt. of ore will
consume 141 b. of iron.

The success of the Clifton artesian bore, on the
Milparinka-Wanaaring track, utterly explodes the old
geologists’ opinion that bores were useless west of the
Paroo. The water is of splendid quality. Now another
bore is to jfcuiown 12 miles out from Milparinka. What
these successes mean few city people have yet half
realised. ♦

Only a month or two ago The Bulletin
read in London Sketch a sparkling interview
with Oscar Wilde—which looked very much as if
it had been written by himself. In it Wilde was
represented as saying: “Exercise! Oh, no, I
never walk—l only talk.” And now they have
put him for six hours daily on the treadmill,
where he must walk and dare not talk. The irony
of it I
Another diving casualty. Diver Woodhead,
working off the Warrior reef, Thursday Island,
was fouled by another man’s pump pipes. Wood-
head disentangled himself promptly,' removed his
diving boots and rose to the surface like a shot,
feet first, the helmet being nearly filled with water
which rnshed'from the lower part of the'dress.
The rapidity and nature of the ascent had para-
lysed the spine, and the man died. Surely this is
a very unusual type of spinal injury.
According to her own evidence, Mrs.
Dean, when her husband was under arrest, got an
order to collect his wages due and another author-
ising her to get his watch and chain. She also
sent to him for his signature (in gaol) an order to
enable her to get possession of his bank-account.
Apparently, however, this cold, business-like pro-
ceeding aggravated Dean, or else he wanted the
money for his defence, for the bank-account wasn’t
handed over.
Dear Bulletin, —You don’t altogether do
justice to Rev. Stone-Wigg, who is more than
fairly liberal for a parson. The Canon’s shot was
something to this effect : That the actual number
of coins in the collections was incompatible with
the size of the congregations. But he also said
that, while the poorer people gave generously, as
a rule—sometimes more, perhaps, than they cen-
veniently could, it was those bedecked in finery
who gave least.
MAXIM.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback- the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation, in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Richard Evans, a tutor, died recently on
the Macleay (N.S.W.), at the age of 94 years. At
an early age he ran away to sea, and served on
board the ship which brought Gov. Macquarie to
N.S. Wales.

talised in “The Parson and the Painter,” has

Both editor Bonython (Advertiser) and
editor Finlayson (Reoister), of Adelaide, have
been honored by Govt, naming “ Hundreds ” and
townships after them. The Hundred of Finlay-
son, situate near Streaky Bay, on the West
Coast, is officially described as “chiefly dense
mallee and tea-tree.”
Premier Kingston has represented West
Adelaide continuously in S.A. Assembly since
March, ’Bl. His father sat in the same House
(one session excepted) from ’57 until his death in
’BO. Never, therefore, since the concession of Con-
stitutional Govt, has a whole year passed without
a Kingston having held a seat in Parliament.
It is rumored in London that the wife of
a certain Judge, who bears a name historical in
Australian legal circles, is about to enter the R.C.
Church. Her husband is said to be quite satisfied
that she should do so, but other members of her
family do not like it at all. This is so far a
secret that people only speak of the lady’s horrible
falling away with bated breath.

Mount Morganaire D’Arcy begins to feel
the effects of London living, and had at latest
been ordered to Marienbad for the waters, and to
give his poor liver a chance. D’Arcy is a model
for all rich Australians in point of modesty and
generosity. Wealth seems not to have spoiled
him, for by all accounts he is the same genial
fellow he ever was in Rockhampton ere Fortune
showered favors upon him.
The shepherding of the best billets in the
Australian Mints for the benefit of political pets
in the House of Commons has come to a tem-
porary stop, by the promotion of Superintendent
Robert Barton to be Deputy-master of Melb. Mint.
George Anderson, M.P. for Glasgow, and Mac-
donald Cameron, rejected M.P. tor somewhere
else, were appointed by Gladstone to the respec-
tive’Mint billets in Melbourne and Sydney.
Pendleton, the new S.A. Railway Com-
missioner, took office June 1, and Smith, Hill and
Neill, his predecessors, are thrown out on the
cold world. He draws .£l2OO a year as compared
with £4500 divided among the former three.

ment of the Vic. railways, and hopes to get a per-
manent post there despite the fact that he offended
H. M. King Syme by giving evidence for Speight
in the late libel-case.

There he fell in love with the matron’s daughter,

The lately-demised Dr. Dobson, M.L.0.,
Vic., had his two wives in one grave, in Melb.
cemetery, with the inscription, “ Mercy and Truth
are met together; Righteousness and Peace have
kissed each other. Psalm 85, 10.” Though
belonging to a literally long-headed family of
Vandemonian plural ists, the harmless Dob.
wrought his own path. His forte was sticking up
for Australian wines, but ho himself seemed to
regard it as being the walking dictionary, the
veritable Privy Council, of lawn-tennis.
Premier Turner has lost all last session’s
anxious look, and now appears about the least per-
turbed man in Vic. Assembly. Yet he has had a
large addition to his regular woik, through the
serious illness of his law-partner. The Premier is
up before seven every morning, into town by 8.15,
and dealing with office and court matters till
noon. Although not a good-looking man, he is a
great ladies’ favorite owing to his patient cour-
tesy. He is the most pestered and politest poli-
tician in the country, and, in addition, the
hardest-working Vic. Premier since the M’Culloch
era.
The absurd Melb. law-fight of Lange v.
Bage has come to an end. By the terms of peace,
Dr. Bage withdraws his notice of appeal for a new
trial in the libel-case which his mother in-law
proved against him, whilst Mrs. Lange exonerates
Bage from the charge of malice and admits his
“correctness” as administrator of the family
estate. Also son Fred. Lange withdraws an action
to remove his brother-in-law from the trusteeship,
and the case of Lange v. Sachse, M.L.O. (another
son-in-law) lapses by general consent. In short,
harmony is restored, and the lawyers have netted
a trifle of £BOOO or so.
John Douglas, Q. Govb. Resident at Thurs-
day Island, addressed 200 Jap. processionists up
there, and, after complimenting them on the way
they smashed the Chinese, called for cheers for
their signal victories. And next he called for cheers
for Li Hung Chang. That is ex-Premier John all
over. When the Paltm r and Charters Towers
diggers rose against the Chinaman, politician John
lauded the opponents about equally, generally de-
voting the larger share of praise to the side with
the greatest number of adherents at the time being.
And on every other question he was about the
same, until no place remained for John but
Thursday Island.

A feminine B.A. is a Coolgardie cook.
And she cooks all the better by reason of being a
B.A.
“The oldest living Australian (white)
native,” Mrs. Bloodworth, has just died. She was
born in 1796, during Hunter’s Governorship, and
was, therefore, only one year short of the century.
She was level-headed to the last, and was con-
nected “way back” with many old N.S.W.
families—notably, the Rankins, Kites, Lees,
Gardiners and other pioneers west of the Blue
Mountains.
Samuel Davenport, with the exception of
Hori Grey, is the oldest surviving member the
Maoriland Crown-colony Council. Grey, as Gov.,
became President of the Council in May, 41;
Davenport joined as a non-official member in
May, ’45 ; his brother, Robert Davenport, became
an elective member in ’5l, in which year G. M.
Waterhouse (who has been Premier both of b.A.
and M.L.) also joined. Save John Rankine, who
dates from ’55, these are the sole surviving poli-
ticians of the days before Constitutional Govt.
Dean Boyle, in his recently-published
reminiscences, has many anecdotes about the late
Professor Badham, of Sydney, of whom he says
that “if a sermon had been wanted in Attic
Greek, Badham would have been equal to the
occasion. I have had long letters from him in his
exile, full of emendations on Plato and Thucy-
dides, and when he met men like Conington at my
house he would pour forth his ideas upon Greek
texts and Shakespeare readings with astonishing
profusion. ”
The gilded Vic. M.L.C., Osmond, 30 years
ago was a furnace-worker at Newington crushing
battery, Stawell, when he bore the nickname of
“ Bill the Smelter.” His “rise” came with the
striking of a rich reef in the North Cross claim, in
which he held 2211 shares, on each of which he
for some time drew a fortnightly div. of £l. This
mine paid enormously for over seven years. Even
when wealthy, Osmond was a familiar figure in
Stawell on his black mare—making tracks from
the butcher’s with meat bought for himself.
Sammy Way is Lieut.-Gov. and Chief
Justice of S.A. ; President of the Executive
Council; Chancellor of the University; Grand
Master of the Masonic Lodge ; Chairman of the
Board of Governors of the Public Library,
Museum, and Art Gallery ; President of the S.A.
Society of Arts ; President of the Children’s
Hospital; Vice-president of the Zoological Society,
and a few other things, besides being a squatter,
a gardener, and a farmer. Yet he never speaks
in connection with any one of the societies with
which he is connected without showing a mastery
of details which would do credit to its principal
executive officer. He has a library of 10,000
books.
The N.S.W. Chief Justice said, the other
day, that there ought always to be police about
the court, “because we never know what might
happen,” There are other Judges who also appa-
rently think that things might happen. For
instance, some time ago a man was being tried cm
a capital charge, and a few minutes before the
lunch-adjournment his lawyer tendered him as a
witness on his own behalf, saying: “ I presume,
your Honor, that the accused will now enter the
witness-box.” “ No,” replied his Honor promptly,
“ let him stay where he is and be sworn.” After
lunch the Judge, evidently seeing that to keep the
witness-prisoner in the dock while giving evidence
would be to leave a big opening for defending
counsel in addressing the jury, told accused that
“ perhaps he had better go into the box.” And
then people noticed that the water and ink-bottles
had been meanwhile cleared off the table, and a
fat detective stationed between his Honor and the
prisoner.

A Sydney solicitor, who does a big divorce
business, was lately offered marriage by a lady

absolute.
Strange things appear to have happened to
the majority of the jurors in a sensational Aus-
tralian case. One man has lost 2st. in weight
through insomnia since the verdict. Another has
taken to drink, and has scarcely had a sober
moment of late. The wife of another has refused
to live any longer with him. A fourth has
developed suicidal tendencies and is being
shadowed by a keeper. A fifth is endeavoring to
recruit his shattered constitution abroad.
When Oscar Wilde got his sentence of
two years’ hard labor a rumor that he had gone
mad within the next fortnight was a foregone
conclusion. Therefore, the odds were a Certainty
to a Probability—tantamount to, say, 15 to Ion —
that the rumor would prove false. Oscar’s alleged
lunacy turned out to be nothing more serious than
the sickness which every intelligent prisoner feels
when subjected to treadmill exercise—the eternal
purposeless “walking up stairs.” When his poor
fat legs showed signs of lameness and his bruised
spirit cried out against the absurdity of his
liver-shaking task, Oscar was put to oakum-
picking for the benefit of his health. At the
present moment, as likely as not, the miserable
aesthete sits in a dungeon cell pulling asunder,
with his white, tapering fingers and broken filbert
nails, the strands of a tarred rope. If he warms
the oakum over his little jet of gas, thereby melt-
ing the tar and taking half the punishment out of
the monotonous job, the warder spots Oscar’s
delinquency and reports him to the prison gover-
nor, who reduces Number So-and-So s allowance
of bread and water for the next fortnight. The
litterateur’s very identity is merged in an arith-
metical designation nowaday.; I'he name that
Oscar Wilde made for himsel. .■ lesn’t officially
exist in his place of captivity. They have num
bered the poet and playwright. Numbered him !

During three years of Chairman Smith’s
term, the S.A. railways (mainly owing to the
prosperity of Broken Hill) returned a handsome
profit after paying interest. No other Aus-
tralian system even pays interest. Smith’s pheno-
menal luck gave out in ’93.
Cambyses flayed an unjust Judge, tanned
the skin and nailed it on the judgment-seat,
each new occupant being sternly told to remember
what he was sitting on. The Frenchmen of the
Revolution tanned the skins of aristocrats and
made leather breeches. Australian wattle-bark
may be required some day for other skins than
cow-hides.

both together £20,000 from a combined popula-
tion of 2| millions, have produced, and must con-
tinue to produce, a badly-employed and badly-
paid “ working ” class.
Cablegram : “ Sir T. Fowell Buxton said
he hoped to foster increased feelings of loyalty in
South Australia. He congratulated Lord Rose-
bery on his success in the Derby on behalf of the
colonists who were fond of racing.” A brighter
remark than the above could be found any day in
the copy-book of a 7-year old school-girl. Why
should we have to suffer such things at breakfast?
They are a slow-going race in Westralia.
For instance, the Ooolgardie telegraph is a single
wire, and to prevent it being blocked by pressure
of traffic, no Westralian newspaper is allowed to
receive more than 500 words a day from the field.
The wire works by day only, hence the pressure ;
the idea of employing a night operator and keep-
ing things going all the 24 hours hasn’t yet
occurred to anybody.
Another leprous Kanaka has just been
discovered on a sugar-plantation at Mackay (Q.)
where the best sugar comes from. N.S. Wales
imports 33,000 tons of sugar from Queensland,
and a large quantity from Hongkong, Fiji and
Java, every grain of which means possible
leprosy. Cane-sugar, in almost every country
on earth save N.S. Wales, is a purely nigger or
Chinese product with possible leprosy in it.

Litligow (N.S.W.), which is represented
by P.M.G. Cook, petitions strongly against Free-
trade. Lithgow lives mostly by industries which
are dependent on Protection, but it voted Free-
trade under the impression-that its own particular
industries were safe from interference, and that
other people’s industries didn t matt l r. N< W, io
its surprise, it finds itself in danger of getting the
thing it voted for, whereas it only intended that
other people should get it.

tralia refuses to join in subsidising the proposed
Pacific cable. South Australia built and main-

of communication with Europe and the rest of the
outer world, and these other provinces now feel
hurt at the unpatriotic selfishness of S.A. in not
helping to support a rival line which will do much

Queensland is endeavoring fco give the
agricultural and pastoral industry a lift by reduc-
ing rail - freights on agricultural produce by
from 20 to 25 per cent, and wool-freights
bv 9 per cent., and is also making reductions
on timber, flour and coal. The Q. railways,
as a whole, already work at a heavy Joss,
and this step is expected to increase that loss
materially. Therefore, the community is to be
taxed to help along certain industries-which is
simply another form of Protection. N.S.W. 1 ree-
traders who advocate reduced freights are re-
quested to consider this aspect of the question.
The N.S.W. Parliament, The Bulletin
says again, could not do without W. I. Crick.
That pugnacious, political gadfly—a man of pro-
nounced parts and pronounced weaknesses—is in
reality the only Court of Appeal apparent to the
public vision, and is the only real and perpetual
Minister for Justice in the province. It takes
such brutal, humanity-awakening, oratorical fero-
city as that shown by him last week on the prison-
gag system to wake up Parliament. If the Reid
Government abolishes the Gag, as implied by the
Premier in reply to the debate thus started, the
credit of the abolition will belong to the devil-saint
Crick, and to nobody else.
The sugar controversy still rages in the
Sydney dailies. One of the latest shriekists on
the subject is a certain “M. S. Hill,” who points
out that N.S. Wales, in ’94, imported 38,000 tons
of sugar, the duty on which, at £5 per ton, was
,£190,000; and grew 25,000 tons, the price of
which was raised <£s a ton by the existence of the
duty. These two amounts make ,£315,000, and
M.S. Hill asks, in a blast of fury : “ For whom
are the whole people taxed this £315,000? Ihc
farmers.” Most people would imagine, from Mr.
Hill’s own figures, that £190,000 of the amount
wasn’t for the farmers or anybody else, but went
to pay the expenses of the Govt., and to save an
equal amount of taxation in some other shape,
but apparently this isn’t so. Meanwhile, it
appears from the Estimates that the sugar-duty
for ’94 didn’t realise £190,000, but that sugar and
molasses together only returned £163,000, or
something has gone wrong with the figures, as
usual. It is difficult to understand how blankly
ignorant of figures is the average economist until
he begins to write to the papers.
Poor M’Ooll, Patterson’s futile and gassy
Minister for Water Supply, was put up to criticise
the programme of the Turner Govt. He was
armed with nearly of notes and waved his
arms indignantly for some time. But his charges
only amounted to a statement that the Ministry
had reduced the allowance for boarding out desti-
tute children from ss. to 4s. per week, that it
should have knocked off one of-the city postal
deliveries, and that it was “inhuman” to press
selectors for arrears of rent. Two poor widows,
he said, had been so badly scared by the demands
for payment that they had sold their cows to raise
' the money demanded by a bowelless Government.
Then the Lands Minister gently asked for the
names and addresses of these widows, so that their
cases might be inquired into and the two poor old
dames letdown as lightly as possible, but M'Goll
refused to supply them, and Best insinuated that
he couldn’t because there had never been any such
widows, and M'Coll sputtered feebly and tried to
use strong language, and then the attack fizzled
out. Altogether it was about the feeblest thing
of its kind upon record.
Premier Reid says that he hopes to build
railways faster than they were ever built before in
New South Wales. If a Betterment law is not
passed, in the first place, this will mean that he
will have to borrow money faster than it was ever
borrowed before to pay for the railways, and more
particularly for the land resumptions, the latter
being generally the biggest item in the bill. Not
one mile of new railway should be commenced
until an efficient Betterment Act is in force.
At the levee held by Vic. Act-
ing-Gov. Madden on Queen’s Birth-
day, France and Russia were repre-
sented by M. Desjardins, the French
Consul. The majesty of the nigger Re-
public of Liberia was on the same
occasion embodied in three consuls.
A conference of Vic. Protec-
tionists lately affirmed the principle
of inserting a minimum wage clause
in the Factories Act. The Bulle-
tin has from time to time referred to
the unspeakable methods of the im-
porter-turned-manufacturer, and has
urged the minimum wage in Protec-
ted Industries as the obvious remedy.
But, more than this, it is a direct
challenge to Freetraders to put Pro-
tection under test conditions, and
prove itself for what it is. No in-
dustry is welcome unless it can pro-
vide its workers with a certain stan-
dard of living, and if Freetraders
are in earnest in their protestations
they will help to legislate this burden
for Protection. If the industries be
extinguished by the enactment, the
Freetraders will have proved their
case —if the industries flourish under
the minimum wage, JJthe Freetraders
shall for ever hold their peace. Amen.
The Maoriland Government
has taken possession of the unfin-
ished Midland railway under the
provisions of the Railway Construc-
tion Act, and, consequently, the pro-
vince’s one lawful experiment in
private land-grant railway budding
should now be near an_ end. The
Midland co. agreed to build 285 miles
of railway, and in consideration of
owning it and taking the profits was
to receive one-half of its cost-price in
land value at 10s. per acre. The co.
built about 90 miles of line, took its
land-errant thereon, and sold it at
13s. 6d. per acre, and then went on
strike for further concessions. It has
been on strike ever since, and at last
the patience of R. Seddon has given
out. The Govt, now proposes to
pay for the four unfinished fragments
of line, and complete them itself,
and swear off land-grants and “ pri-
vate enterprise.”
g.A. Govt, has given notice of the ve-
gumption of 98 sq. miles of land held by Mr. J.
Bosworth, an ex-legislator. It is to be cut up
into farms in time for next seeding season
In a list of J.’sP. gazetted in Adelaide
May 30, were the names of E. Alcock, chief
executive officer of the Rechabites, and of G.
Flecker, a prominent hotel-keeper. All things
to all men.”

ASK VO UR DRUGGIST.
Society
Australian girls who want to marry titles
need not go abroad to look for them any longer.
There are several bachelor-knights locally avail-
able. Zeal, the new Vie. knight, is a bachelor,
Ex-Premier Patterson is a widower, and is con-
sidered vtry eligible now that he has arrested the
tendency to baldness which threatened to devas-
tate his head, some time ago. What offers?
« • •
, The beauty competition in Melb.—prize,
a gold cup—in which Grace Pa!otta carried off
the prize, is remembered, writes a lady to The
Bulletin, as the “Grace Palottery.” It was the
men whose votes awarded Grace the cake. Her
fascinating manners and piquant action, rather
than lur mere looks, won their hearts. The
women dispute the verdict wildly, and adduce it
as an evidence of the unreliability of men’s judg-
Photo, by Vandyck.
Miss GERTRUDE JERRARD, another Melb.
girl with an alleged voice.
ment when women’s beauty is concerned. They
do not judge purely by faces, but by manner,
temperament, and all sorts of other things, men-
tionable and otherwise. The cold perfection of
Galatea would “stand no show” beside the
vivacity of a French opera-bouffe artist, who,
though less beautiful, kicks high, and shows rows
of pearly teeth when she smiles.
Parson Jas. Clarke, C. of E. incumbent at
Kogarah (N.S.W.)—so tender in love and so
dauntless in war! —is now battling with his Bishop,
lest his preaching-license be taken from him.
Clarke, having been divorced (pending appeal)
from his wife, is episcopally deemed too human to
remain a parson ; but the Bishop has reserved
judgment, principally on the ground that, until
leave to appeal is refused, the divorce-case is still
sub-judice. Whatever the event, Clarke is a
likely man to fight the issue on the most Quixotic
chances. The Church Temporalities Act will be
tested, and the powers of ecclesiasticism in Aus-
tralian courts weighed to the last grain. Parson
Clarke was the only O. of E. clergyman who
visited in prison Tom Walker, when that agnostic
celebrity shot poor Parson Laseron. Clarke even
found bail for Walker. But “when I was un-
justly detained in a lunacy asylum,” says Clarke,
“no brother clergyman visited me to comfort or
solace me!” Clarke is too human to remain a
parson.
• # #
An amusing scene is enacted at the N.S.W.
Treasury on the Ist of every month. A retired
school-teacher calls for his pension, but will not
give up the receipt for same till he has the cheque
in his hand ; “ because,” he piously observes, “it
will not be honest for me to give a receipt for
Bomething I have not yet received, and ” —more
practically—“ you could keep the receipt, and say
I had been paid.” A clerk, therefore, has to hold
the cheque, while the ex-school-teacher holds the
receipt, and when the latter says “ Now ! ” they
grab for each other’s documents.
» * *
Capfc. Close, the N.S.W. soldier who
came into prominence recently through having
loved not wisely but too well, wishes to issue a
writ claiming .£5OOO damages from Major-General
Hutton. And the Major-General wishes the
Crown Solicitor to accept service— i.e., lie wishes
the country to take, the responsibility of the mis-
takes if any, committed in his official capacity.
The issue seems plain enough. If Gen. Hutton
took the action complained of without authority
from his Ministerial head (as alleged), then the
Gen. must take the responsibility. If otherwise,
the responsibility rests with Govt.
About a year ago the now-released Sydney
boom-defaulters petitioned for the remission of
what then remained of their term of gaol. In the
petition, they, or their friends, were injudicious
snough to suggest that their conviction was due to
the prevailing panic ; and further, that their sen-
tences wore, for the same reason, more severe than
those usual insuchcases. This riled the Judge, who
had both approved of the verdict and awarded the
sentence, and he sent in a minute so crushing that
even the influence which Abigail could bring to
bear took nearly a year to overcome its effect.

Should everything be brought to light in
she now-notorious Butler (Sydney) case the dis-
closures will shake the very foundations of local
society. And it is distinctly in the interests of
honesty and the impartial administration of justice
that nothing of this hideous affair f ; ild be con-
cealed,
* « •
Brother Windeyer’s phenomenal luck
threatens to turn. Several recent cases of his
seem to be going the wrong way. The Dean case,
in which he averred he was as certain of accused’s
guilt “ as if he had actually seen him administer
the poison,” is certainly looking rather upside-
down ; and in the Clarke divorce-case, the C. of
E. ecclesiastical court failed to find Parson Clarke
guilty of drunkenness—of which the Civil Court
was fully assured. In the Butler v. Butler suit,
wherein Windeyer J. committed Mrs. Butler’s
brother (subsequently convicted) for perjury, two
people have now been committed for conspiring to
make it appear that the witness had perjured
himself.
# * *
A recent case of arsenical-poisoning fol-
lowing the Dean inquiry recalls the d ith of
Unwin, the Glebe (Sydney) eoal-merchai.t, some
time ago, through the medium of arsenic put into
a flask of brandy left “carefully” in his buggy.
The murderer was never discovered.
# * #
The Bulletin’s own special clairvoyant
predicts that the report of the Dean Commission
will be to the effect that it has taken a large
amount of new evidence, and finds there is reason-
able doubt as to the guilt of Dean, whose release
it recommends. Nothing will be said about the
absolute wickedness of the methods adopted at
the trial, and no comment will be made on the
presiding Judge’s theory that there were only two
alternative theories possible—either (1) that Dean
Was the poisoner, or (2) that one of the women
concerned did the deed—the theory (to some
extent backed up by expert medical evidence)
that very likely the woman was never poisoned at
all, having thus been quite overlooked.
# # *
One thing, anyhow, is certain ; That the
public is emphatically and overwhelmingly of
opinion that it is for the Crown to prove Dean
guilty, and not for Dean to prove his innocence.
The more than reasonable doubt which existed as
to the man’s innocence when the Commission of
Enquiry was appointed has been enormously
increased by the revelations as to the low-down
associations of the women concerned—to say
nothing of the disclosures made concerning the
rioketiness of the medical evidence, as reported
at the trial.
* * *
The Crown must have been hard-up to
asperse alleged-poisoner Dean’s character when it
puts in an absolutely illegal conviction recorded
against him in his infantile days. When 13 years
old he “soldiered” a horse, and, in default of
paying the fine, served three months in gaol.
Poor little wretch ! The 13-year-old boy who
would not soldier a horse, if he had half a chance,
is no true-born Australian.

It is gradually dawning upon a few usually
well-informed Australian business men that
there’s a lot of method in what was described as
the madness of a oertiiu Northern “merchant”
when he fitted up a portion of his suite of offices
as a luxurious drawing-room in which they were
entertained with cigars and wine ad lib. As at
least one foreign merchants friends at home have
bitter reason to know, their host is purely and
simply the secret political agent of a foreign
power.
# • #
It is unfortunate that the defence was not
able to call the clairvoyante lady as a witness at
Dean’s trial ac Darlinghurst, as her evidence
would probably have had considerable weight
with the Judge. There would have then been a
conflict of clairvoyancy, for the Judge, as it were,
saw a fair man putting poison in his wife’s food,
and the other possessor of supernatural sight saw
a dark man as the cause of all the trouble. Bub
the amateur wizard would surely have given way
to the professional.
• * *
■ The story goes that Dr. Knaggs’ intended
anti-spiritualistic exposition at the N.S.W.
Women’s Loan Exhibition fell through, on the
ground that the proposed show was of a nature
calculated to offend the Cissy and Josephine
brigade. If The Bulletin mistakes not, Dr.
Knaggs is the medico who was, some_ years ago,
publicly challenged by a Sydney spiritist to give a
performance of the nature indicated. It is a pity
that his offer should have lapsed.
* * *
Pilcher, Q.C., complains, and with reason,
of the manner in which the N.S.W, Crown Law
office is conducting the Dean Enquiry. The
matter is being fought, on the part of the Crown,
with all the bitterness of a contested action, and
with all the desperation of a defence. The Crown
Law offices are determined to wipe away the
implied slur upon their administration, and the
police, knowing this, see promotion in the ruin of
Dean, and are tempted to act accordingly.
HIS LATEST PICTURE.
“The faculty” adores terrible names.
Through the Dean Poisoning Commission! the
public knows more about “peripheral neuritis”
than it knows about influenza ; and yet, a
month ago, not one reader in 10,000 had
ever heard of such an awful thing. Which
awful thing is merely a symptom of several
“complaints,” and every hardened old sot who
has “ suffered ” a prolonged “recovery” has pro-
bably had it a hundred times. “ Wot’s er matter,
ole f’ler?”< )“ Got the cramps in my toes, knees,
and hands.” “Cramps? By Jove! old man,
you’ve got peripheral neuritis!” Then the har-
dened old sot will try and recollect a prayer, and
give up his drunken-ghost.
* * *
“You see, I never receive gentlemen,”
was the remark of the female clairvoyant who
gave evidence before the Dean Commission last
week. Just so. It is the women who rush the
CAUGHT IN THE ACT.
Wicketkeeper (to umpire) : “How's that? ”
PROFESSOR BASALT (who has been persuaded to
play, and has stepped out to a twister):
“Look here, young man , I saw you do
that yourself. ”
diviners and futurists, and not only the vulgar
and illiterate females, either. Lovely woman has
such a stock of superabundant curiosity that
things mundane, from A to Z, fail to satisfy her,
and peer she must into the supposed supernatural.
When palmistry fails, she goes for clairvoyancy ;
that worked out, to the common “cutting of the
cards ” —three rows of the “ mystic nine” and five
over for indices of prognostication—with the yarn
about the fair man and the dark worked out by
“rules of contrairies.” The cards being satisfied,
she will live to be further fooled by the mere dregs
of a brew of tea in a cup, just as ancient priestly
soothsayers used to build auguries from the entrails
of an ox. Bah! Humanity progressive? Not as
to superstition ;it merely changes its vogues. All
the same : Short advice to fortune-tellers —“Never
receive gentlemen.”
In one particular, the Dean-case fortune-teller
was strikingly schismatioal from the usual
“patter” of her craft. Ninety-nine clairvoyants
in a hundred, of the vulgar fortune-telling sort,
and even trance-lecturers and alleged medical
diagnosers, will tell you that they “ never remem-
ber what they saw or said in ‘ that state.’ ” Never-
theless, that is nob always true of the true
trance or catalepsy. No less authorities
than Peter, Paid and Patmoa John and all
the ancient seers and orthodox cranks prove that
much abundantly. And so it is alleged by the
more enlightened fin-de-siecle “ psychics ” who are
neither the “flesh” of spiritualists, nor the
“ fowl ” of the theosophists, but merely their own
doubtful sort of “red-herring.” An alcoholic
cateleptic always remembers his “visions,” but,
if he doesn’t make a note of them right on the
point of waking, five minutes will often bury
them in oblivion for ever.
# * *
The American “Transportation Commis-
sion ” has come to Australia. This is indeed a
most insulting circumstance.
The centre of Europe is the great
suicide land. There are very few suicides
among the mountains, bub in the low-
lands they are plentiful, principally along
the banks of large rivers. In swampy
regions their numbers diminish. _ More
suicides take place in June than in any
other month, and less in December. As
many suicides take place in the first 10
days of each month as in the last 20.
Men generally kill themselves on Mon-
days, Tuesdays, and Thursdays (Wednes-
day—always selected by the Jews for
weddings—for some reason or other, is
an off-day, and after Thursday man is
probably kept up by the knowledge that
he will get his wages on Saturday).
Women generally suicide on Sunday,
most likely through the misery of the old
man being at home that day.
Which reminds The Bulletin of some-
thing. Mr. and Mrs. Dean, of the
mysterious Sydney poisoning-case, must
have been together by far too much for
their mutual happiness—anyhow, Dean
must have been far too much in the way,
no matter how gotd and level-tempered
his wife may have been. He was in
command of the night_ ferry-boat, the
only means of communication between
North Shore, where their home was, and
Sydney, and so he had his wife, as it were,
almost under lock-and-key. And as he
was up all night he was at home all day.
They might ha\e been happy had they
lived in Sydney —without his mother-in-
law.
The latest, and least, of Melbourne’s
amateur vocalists to take a benefit for the purpose
of going ’Ome to study is Miss Gertrude Jerrard.
Her farewell concert at the Town Hall was well
attended, so Miss Jerrard will doubtless stare for
London forthwith, and place her organ under
Santley’s care. It is understood that Santley has
kindly undertaken to do his best for the young
lady. He was very friendly with her father in
days of yore, which seems to explain the baritone’s
otherwise unaccountable admiration for his old
friend’s daughter’s singing powers. To the
writer’s way of thinking, Miss Jerrard is very i n-
likely to “ knock ” Europe either with her t( p
notes or her comic opera abilities, and the writer
speaks as one who has suffered a rendering of
Serpolette (in “ Cloches de Corneville ”) at her
fair young hands. This, however, is no reason
why Miss J. shouldn’t go home to study. Unfor-
tunately, her triumphant departure is likely to
unsettle the minds of dozens of other young
amateurs having equal pretensions to notice.
Madame Belle Cole’s testimony to Miss Gertrude
Jerrard’s musical prospects was rather ambiguous,
“ Before many years I hope to hear that she has
secured some kind of position in the old world.”
» * *
Since the scandal about the mysterious
disappearance of the Yic. Parliamentary mace,
extraordinary precautions have been taken to
protect the present gilded wooden bauble. It is
“planted” every evening after the House uses,
in a part of the cellar of the building known only
to the officer who has 'special charge of it, and is
solemnly dragged forth again about a quarter of
an hour before the House sits. Not even Mr.
Speaker knows where it is kept. An indiscreet
reporter who jocularly asked a question about it
one day, was eyed with the gravest suspicion, and
has since been made to feel like a marked man.
The mace is still a painfully sore subject with the
Parliamentary officials.
* * *
Melbourne johnnies, having gained experi-
ence at last Actors’ Charity Carnival, this time
mostly took the precaution to provide themselves
with small change. One gilded youth took a
friend to a refreshment-bar, asked for two drinks,
and pub down a shilling. “What’s this for?”
said the Thespian behind the bar, looking scorn-
fully at the “ bob.” “ Two drinks,” said the cus-
tomer. The actor-man answered never a word,
but solemnly drew the cork from a bottle of
Pommery, and handed it over, remarking, “ ’Ere,
help yourselves. I couldn’t insult you with any-
thing else.”

“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of theHoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has tak n
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and E. It is certainly the funnit st Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “ Killaloe ” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing lor an aspir-
ing comique, pro. or amateur."

# * *
The complaint made re the exclusion of
(unaristocratic) invalid carriages from Sydney
Botanic Gardens on band-days, recalls the fact
that some years ago a man who, while sitting on
a garden-seat with his invalid wife, placed one
arm around her waist, was summoned for in-
decency, or riotous conduct, or something.
• * •
The recent decision of a Sydney court that
kissing in public cannot be legally described as
“ indecent behaviour,” is in the teeth of another
Sydney court decision of 1876, where two youths
were fined under that indictment for kissing a girl
at Belmore market.
* • •
Mrs. Dean, of Winton (M.L.), in whose
garden the remains of three deceased infants were
lately dug up, is described by her neighbors as the
good Samaritan of the district—a woman who
was always ready to assist everybody in distress
to the utmost of her ability. Also, she was a
good-looking woman of considerable refinement,
and had a poetic craze for flowers, her flower-
garden being one of the show places of the district.
The blossoms grew well, no doubt, on account of
the dead baby at the roots. Somehow the lady
who has dead infants found in her garden is
generally a kindly person who is always will-
ing to help an afflicted fellow-creature on his
path, and the more kindly she is the mere
dead’ infants are found in her garden. So
far, the police believe that they have traced
22’children to Mrs. Dean’s possession, and
of these eight are still unaccounted for, though
every vestige of the lovely garden has been torn
up by the roots in the search for more remains.
The. ten living children who were found on the
premises all apparently well-cared for and
religiously brought up, and on the mantelpiece
was a neatly-framed In Memoriam poem, written
by the baby-farmer herself, expressing her grief at
the death of one of her charges—a little girl who
was found planted beneath a chrysanthemum.

A single girl, afflicted with a baby, drowned
herself in a quarry-hole at Ballarat last week, A
man, who was working near the hole, gave evi-
dence at the inquest. He said he had seen the
body of a woman floating in the water, bub made
no effort to get it out, thinking he might fall into
trouble. When eventually the body was brought
to hand, he was horrified to find it was that of his
own sister.
# * #
There was much that was jarringly in-
artistic in the deliberate display made of Violet
Varley’s funeral. The advertising of the route,
the stopping at the church, and the parade of
floral offerings whetted the popular taste for sen-
sationalism, and the result was a public exhibition
of the most vulgar curiosity The church was
packed with weeping women whose hysterics
naturally unnerved the chief mourners and made
them the centre of a scene of almost bathetic
grief. All along the line of procession, too,
stupid' women could be seen breaking away from
the crowd to trot alongside the mourning-coach
and peer in upon tenor Tapley, just to see
and say how he took his terrible trouble.
Nothing more boorishly brutal could be imagined.
Then again, the four-horsed hearse, gorgeously
plumed and appointed, the conspicuous outbreak
of mourning-coach, and the other costly provisions
of the undertaker’s art, proved a motley contrast
to the variety of strange vehicles which fol-
lowed. There seemed comparatively few well-
known faces amongst the mourners, who, for the
most part, appeared to comprise every whipper-
snapper of a city clerk who possessed the price of
a cab-fare and could get a few hours off the chain.
Mixed up as the cortege was with a State function,
a butchers’ procession, and a fashionable wedding
—State, stage, slaughter-house, and society I—the
ostentatious funeral of the poor little actress was
a garish affair, which made the poetic grieve
that it had not been decided to have a simple
burial at early morn, without a funeral oration,
without a mob of women and children already
surfeited with the sight of decorated meat-carts,
without a ton or so of wreaths, without anything
discordant whatever, and with only a few real
mourners, content to grieve unseen, and satisfied
with placing a humble violet or two upon the little
grave. # * *
Sydney D.T, permits, in its columns, a
woman to swallow 2oz. of strong solution of
ammonia (that would burn holes in a nickel-
stomached dipsomaniac), then to converse with
her husband, to swallow an emetic (!), and to
linger languorously in an up-country hospital.
The Bulletin is proud of Australian women, but
this is “ tough, sir, devilish tough,”

As a result of the Standard Time Act the
S.A. Govt, has robbed public servants of half an-
hour a week. It has opened the offices at 9
Photo, by Lon. Stereoscopic Co.
LORD BRASSEY, the new Vic. Governor .
instead of 9.30, closing at 4.30 instead of 5 on
ordinary days, but retaining noon as the closing
time on Saturdays. The change is made to save
gas in the winter evenings.

ft has" in fact, become common talk in educa-
tional circles that youths who fear to face the
■ ity exam, go (like “rocky” life-assurance pro-
onents) to the country, and pass creditably.

The Oriental hawker has developed a new
phase of usefulness in Tasmania, one of the
brotherhood having been served with a citation as
co-respondent in divorce. Plaintiff and respondent
are well-known Tas. residents.
# * *
As thousands of unemployed have been
struck off the books of N.S.W. Friendly Societies
for not paying up, a benevolent movement is on
foot to keep such unfortunates financial for the
future, and thus save them and their families
from leaning on public charities when sickness
attacks them.

The Church office, which controls the
Anglican trust-funds in Adelaide diocese, owns
three hotels. That denomination therefore must
tolerate Gov. Buxton, even although he is a
brewer,

LADY BRASSEY .
The Melb. man charged with having sold
his interest in a certain estate to from two to
thirteen different persons, was released by the
Bench on general principles. The point at which
the Bencli knocked off hearing the case and threw
accused out into the street was where it tran-
spired that accused had sold his interest allegedly
for the severalth time—for =6200. The man who
advanced the money, in addition to getting what,
if it hadn’t been sold before, would have been a
very valuable property, for a mere song, appears to
have got .£2O for his trouble. A man who intro-
duced the seller to him, and another man who
acted as runner-up, got £45 between them. This
nominally left £135, but when the accused was
found in a sort of moonstruck condition by the
police very shortly afterwards, he had only 9d.,
and as his breath didn’t account for the balance
the Bench concluded that it also went in commis-
sion to somebody. So the case was thrown down
the steps on principles which might not be good
law, but which seem to be very respectable jus-
tice, anyhow.
# • •
Mr. Justice Simpson has been presiding in
N.S.W. Divorce Court. Why the eternal fitness
of' things is thereby illustrated The Bulletin
cannot exactly define, but that his Honor should
preside at the Divorce Court is plain to the
meanest intelligence. It would be as pleasant to
be divorced by his Honor, if you had to be
divorced, as to be arrested by the polite and sym-
pathetic detective Rochaix, if you had to be
arrested.

drop without the other. When two men are tied together

Alfred Barham Black ; (1) The “ University staffs of
Britain ” are not “unanimous” in favor of bi-metallism.
(2) “ We know that wages have not been raised in silver-
using lands.” Well, they certainly have been raised in
some silver lands to the present writer’s knowledge. (3)
The statement that wages won’t rise in our time in silver
lands is foolish. No one knows what will happen in our
time, or in anyone else’s time. (4) You say “prices rose
between 1850 and 1870, and fell in 1870’90.” Not quite
correct. Agricultural produce rose —it did the same, by
the way, to a much greater extent in 1780-1820. Manu-
factured goods tither tell or remained stationary. (5) In
your garbled quotations as to the increase in the volume
of money, you conveniently leave paper quite out of the
reckoning....R.H.R. (Hobart); Not up to high-water

The human body is a victim to those
dreadful enemies, the microbes, and to prevent
those treacherous foes to approach you there is no
better method than to keep yourself clean and not
allow food to decay in your mouth or the clogging
of the epidermis. To prevent these things, one of
the most leading chemists in Sweden brought out
his world-renowned Amykos, which, without
doubt, is the best toilet requisite with which to
gargle your throat, wash your mouth and face.
Amykos is strongly recommended as a preventa-
tive of sore throat and bad breath.
A Parramatta gaol convict has got six
months’ coercion for smuggling in a copy of
Sunday Times. An offence like that should be
allowed to bring its own punishment.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The monster benefit matinee, tendered as a fare-
well compliment to Mme. Soldene, at Sydney
Lyceum, last week, was not only a great success
from the bag point of view, but it was a wondrous
demonstration of the triumph of temperament over
the man with the scythe, Madame is the Parkes
of the operatic stage—the grand'old woman of
light opera. Her voice is merely the echo of its
old self, and Lady Jane’s (in “Patience”) inven-
tory of her “ spreading charms ” is not altogether
unapplicable to Madarne’s personality—yet, by
reason of her marvellous vitality and her sound
artistio methods, she still makes acceptable stage-
appearances, and kicks out a long pink train with
her French heel with all the skittisbness of two-
and-twenty. At least, so she did in “Madame
Angot,” at her ben., when she skipped through
the part of Mdlle. Lange, in a melting rose-color
dress, a big feather fan, and a luxuriant brown
wig, and filled several weary young women in the
dress-circle with the fervent hope that they would
show the same happy vigor at 37 which Madame
does at—shall I say, double that ?
The matinee was brought to a successful issue
with the ready help of a long army of artists, in-
cluding the ’Varsity amateurs—whom the wicked
gallery saw fit to guy most unmercifully. A
feature of the Soldene affair was the absence of
the Grateful Person who slides out of the wings
as the audience gets its wrap together and thanks
people “in heartfelt tones ” for being so kind as
to spend an afternoon in pursuit of amusement.
It is quite time the benefit-speech was put a stop
to j at least, it is quite time to stop thanking the
public for turning up. The only people who
ought to be warmly puffed are the actors and
actresses, who certainly deserve all the advt, they
can get out of giving their services and ensuring a
bright, attractive bill, and who are really respon-
sible tor big houses on these cordial occasions. If
you don’t believe it just watch the fate of some
“deserving object” benefit in which small fry
only take part. It’s not the beneficiary which
fetches the audience—it’s the bill.
Talking of benefits, Miss Nellie Stewart’s well-
deserved turn was billed for 12th inst., too late for
me to notice this week, but a full house is a fore-
gone conclusion. Nellie, sticking to what she
told us when she returned from ’Orae, sails back
to grasp once more at British laurels, and if the
British public know a bright, refined, and charm-
ing actress when they see her, and recognise a
sweet, expressionful soprano when they hear it,
and can respect an infinite capacity for hard work
and a determination never to disappoint the
public—then one may safely predict a walk-over
for Miss Stewart with anything like a fair chance.
Wiseacres from over the dew-drop may shake
their heads knowingly and infer that little bundles
of beauty and talent like Nellie hang on every
bush “in town,” but it seems rather odd they
don’t import them oftener instead of sending out
hopeless sticks who get strained half-way through
the season. Nellie’s departure is Florence Young’s
opportunity. I hear that the well-dressed Miss
Young will be often seen in leading r6les when
Nellie packs.
It is difficult to keep pace with the Most
Travelled’s celebrities nowadays—they tread on
one another’s heels so. The only thing I didn’t
like about Mr. Haskett-Smith (the latest) was his
hyphen ; otherwise he was a geographical and
antique treat. It is a fine thing to hear an Eng-
lish parson graciously enlightening our aboriginal
ignorance on the Holy Land without giving
exhausted “ haws ” at every comma or appearing
too feeble in the jaw to send his voice further than
the sixth row, or calling languidly on the
“Loard.” I heard Mr. Smith’s illustrated con-
versation on “ Bible Memories,” and must confess
to having passed a pleasant evening, though, mind
you, Mr. Smith is not a ladies’ man. Every
woman-anecdote he related made the eternal she
look ridiculous ; and the list culminated in a word-
picture of the New Woman on of the
Dead Sea wearing knickers, a boiled shirt, a flam-
ing red tie, and a velvet coat. Even the Bedouins
shied at her.
Mr. Smith’s photographs are exceedingly in-
teresting (anybody who wants to see the source of
the Jordan has only to go to the head of Lane
Cove River—if Mr. Smith’s snap-shots are to be
relied on), and there isn’t the least suspicion of
snivel or cant in his boldly-delivered discourse. I
think he must be the New Parson, who will have
a magic-lantern rigged up in the choir, and preach
to crowded houses with a bamboo pointer and a
white sheet, Mr. S. tells some funny stories,
which are the sort of jokes nobody but a clergy-
man, or a respectable daily paper, could tell with-
out damaging their reputation for piety. Here is
one. A hotel in Jaffa is kept by a “ character ”
who names his two wings the Old and New Testa-
ment wing, respectively ; and there are 12 mmns
in each named after the 12 tribes and the dozen
apostles. “It is interesting,” innocently said the
rev. lecturer, “ to hear mine host telling a servant
to show some lady up to Peter 1"
The only people who had a grievance at the
Fresh Air League ball (at Sydney Town Hall, the
other evening) were a handful of men whose black
coats were covered in fluff from the pampas-grass
used in the decorations. Otherwise the hall-trim-
ming was a great success—ferns in profusion, and
banana-trees, palms, and other herbs, with an under-
current of quarantine-yellow art muslin, made the
ball-room look its very best. The cosy corners
were a feature—some of them fenced in with
Japanese screens would have made ideal spooning-
placesifonly they hadn’t been arranged to accom-
modate six people ! Surely ball-committees know
that “Two is company’’ and t6te-it-t6te corners
should be built for two and not half-a-dozen.
The dressing wasn’t bad at all. The Acting-
Governoress (His Acting Ex. didn’t come) was
enshrined on the well-draped dais, and was well-
draped herself in black brocade and lace with
green-velvet sleeves, a diamond necklace and pen-
dant, the whole livened up by committee flowers.
Miss Darley wore amber satin, and her sister a
dull-looking black-and-bluish gown. Mrs. Hutton
sported blue brocade and filmy lace like cobwebs
on a clear sky—and unwisely chaperoned a thin
girl in bright green, by the way, who really
looked like a French bean on end. Mrs. Rupert
Carington sported a dolly dress of blue satin
fussed over with blue tulle and blue velvet bows.
One of the dreamiest dresses on view was worn
by Mrs. Willie Mitchell—pearly brocade with
subdued embroideries and immense clusters of
pink roses on either shoulder and at the foot of
the skirt. _ Mrs. John See wore trained ivory
brocade striped with pale spring green, the bodice
a mass of ivory lace and diamonds; Miss Marcia
Cox, in a simple black skirt with green velvet
bodice fitfully trimmed with old lace and little
bunches of violets stuck round the neck and
waist, looked artistic ; and Miss M'Donagh’s black
silk frock, brightened with flat pink velvet bows,
was most stylish. Mrs. Caro (hon. see.) wore
strawberry brocade-pink velvet and diamonds;
Mrs. Woodin (the hard-working hon. sec.) wore a
well-cut brocade; Mrs. W. Brennan, in pink
poplin, had a color-match in Essie Jenyns-Wood,
who also wore pink, with a jewelled ceinture h, la
Juliet. By the way, the “best people” didn’t
wear the off-the-shoulder sleeve. I thought they
wouldn’t.
Of course, there were some sartorial eccentrici-
ties on the crowded floor. One girl wore white
and green, with green splashboards over each
shoulder, and a head-arrangement of magenta
flowers from her crown over her right ear, and
thence round her neck ! The misguided lady evi-
dently mistook herself for a verandah - post.
Another charmer, bundled up in scarlet, had
evidently never seen Katisha, whom she exactly
resembled; and yet another sat with two gold-
washed shoes stuck out, looking as if she had just
walked through a Coolgardie gold-mine and some
of the color had stuck. Also, a lady in a red
bodice and petticoat had a most wonderful train
of black velvet with big, ugly, pink roses here and
there, as if stuck on transfer-fashion. How a
woman who wilfully bedizens her back-view with
a horrible design like that must envy a peacock
his tail-feathers ! Pink and blue were greatly
worn, and a visitress from Melbourne, in a forked-
lightning sort of frock, sported the very latest
notion—a gold muff-chain—round her neck. But
toNellie Stewart belongs whatever credit apper-
tains to introducing ’em into Australia.
I thought a mourning-veil hung over “La
Cigale,” at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday, when
the grasshopper opera went up. Nobody could
help thinking of poor, pretty Yi. Varley
who last played the fusty part of Marton
for us (she really sang all the summer and
died in the winter like the grasshopper in the
fable) and the appearance of the ever-ready Bracy
in Tapley’s part, as the Chevalier, helped to keep
one reminiscency throughout the piece. “Cigale”
was brilliantly staged, and Miss Stewart gave an
entirely new and charming reading of the name-
part. What may be described as the gooseflesh
effects introduced into the last verse of “One
between Three ” resulted in that quaint ditty
having to be repeated three times, while the
dramatic scene in the last act has never before
been so tellingly rendered—Miss Stewart’s artistic
pallor very much helping the effect. I liked Nellie
as La Cigale.
The dressing was most aggressive after the first
act. In act 2 Miss Stewart sported honey-color
satin helped out with white and gold tissue, a big
white hat with white and silver feathers, and a
pair of full brothers to “ O dem golden slippers,' 1
immortalised in minstrelsy, on her tiny feet. Flo
Young, as the Duchess, ran about the stage in old
gold striped brocade, with a mottled-lobster petti-
coat, and a big brown hat with maroon roses and
brown feathers. Miss Young is a very fine figure
of a girl, but even Juno irritates you when she
tears about the stage in the aimless hurry of a
just-set-a-going mechanical toy, which is a fault
Miss Young has lately fallen into. The men have
a shy at silks and satins in “Cigale,” In act 2
Bracy, of the sweet, reliable tenor, wore a splendid
satin suit profusely trimmed with shoe-laces, and
Vernon, as the Duke, was very gorgeous, only
that his frivolous handkerchief was three sizes too
large for the tra-la-la occasion. One looked for a
lacey trifle, not a head-cold-bandanna, under the
frisky circumstances. Apropos of the flinty duke,
that largely-patronised children’s party he gives
looks a most probable sort of entertainment for a
duke of his proclivities to inaugurate.
In the last act, Miss Young was rigidly elegant
in eau-de-nil brocade, with huge bunches of pink
flowers over a pink skirt, veiled in coffee-color
lace, pink roses, and diamonds by the spoonful.
Nellie Stewart’s gown was a white brocade,
out Juliet-wise, thickly ruched at both ends
with yellow roses, which also formed arm-
lets; a draped train from the right shoul-
der of pale green, embossed in gold and
caught with yellow roses, was lined with yellow
satin. Diamonds to conclude. Dost like the pic-
ture? I did. With every apology to Lauri (who
made his usual success of the Uncle), I think the
funniest thing occurred in the Ist act. “Como
along, children,” chirped the only Nellie, “and
whoever’s first gets the pick of the plums! ”
Thereupon May Pollard (the “ Ant”) is heard to
groan, “Then good-bye to the cherry tree ! ”
Evidently tigs grow on thistles in Scotland. Sid
Deane was Vincent (“Vonsong ” in the first act,
but “Viscount” afterwards) in place of idol
Brownlow, and people groaned in spirit when he
turned up to make love to Nellie, though it was
satisfactory, in one way—it made the lady’s persis-
tent refusal of his unmodulated affection look the
most natural thing on earth.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The shearers and other bush-workers in
Wilcannia district (where more than usually
serious work was done during last shearing) have
expressed an emphatic opinion about the latest
A.W.U. manifesto, which practically authorised
local agreements. They censure the Executive,
declare that it has lost the confidence of members,
denounce the manifesto as cowardly, and resolve
to insist upon conference or satisfactory verbal
agreement under conference rules. It is no use
blinking the fact: scores of men in that and the
neighboring river district have been preparing for
a row all the time.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their

“ That which we call a rose, by any other
name would smell as sweet,” and so it
happens that we want to explain what
is MYFA? It is only a name,
the new name for Mitchell’s
“My Favorite” Tea. Why do
we change the name ? Simply to
protect you from fraud. The success of
“My Favorite” Tea has been
gigantic ; its immense popularity is so
pronounced that it has numbers of imita-
tors already, who have neither the brains
nor the inclination to get an original
name of their own
A recent decision of the Supreme Court has revealed the fact that the
words “ My Favorite ” can be used by anybody, and we have therefore, in
order to prevent your being misled into buying “Favorite” Tea, and
thinking it the celebrated blend of Mitchell’s, decided to change the name
for one that cannot be held by anyone else. .
MITCHELL’S “MY FAVORITE” Tea will be sold in future under the
name of MYFA TEA (copyright).
We positively guarantee “ MYFA TEA ” to be the identical Tea hitherto
sold as MITCHELL’S “MY FAVORITE.” What’s in a Name P A good
deal. Be warned! In future, when you wish to purchase MITCHELL’S
“MY FAVORITE” Tea, ask for
MYFA
EASILY EEMEMBEEED AS THE FIEST FOUE LETTEES OF
MY FAVORITE.

Dear Bulletin, —There are other than
bank-managers selling saveloys. Fancy four ex-
station-managers meeting as boundary-riders in
the corners of their respective paddocks to com-
pare notes. The crows watching the scene from
the posts of the diverging fences added the neces-
sary touch of Bananaland pathos.—R.A.F. Q.

Dear Bulletin, —Sydney had better get
along without a Chinese detective. Melbourne
had one—Book Shing—who, for all valuable pur-
poses, proved a failure. He was useful enough as
an interpreter, but, when it came to real business,
he was as much fogged as the Caucasian D. It
might have been different had he not become
Anglicised in Beaufort coat, tweed trousers, &c.;
as soon as he did, ho lost that touch with his
fellow Mongols which is essential to the investi-
gator of Chinese crime. Unless Sydney can get
a Chow content to remain a Chow and act the D.
at the same time, it’s no use making any experi-
ment.—Yours, Cop.

A Dunedin (M.L.) debt-collector was lately
taken down by a Chinaman. He had to collect a
debt of <£l from one (say) Too Kwid, greengrocer.
He had called repeatedly at Too Kwid’s, but was
told T.K. was “ ill,” or “just gone out,” and was
going to prepare a summons. But one morning a
knowing Celestial called on the collector, and made
known to him that he could get in T.K.’s money
if the collector would allow him something for his
trouble—say, give him a receipt for 20s. and accept
13s. He also promised to see the matter settled in
a week’s time, but it might take longer, as Too
Kwid - was a hard nut. The collector agreed he
would take anything rather than sue a Chinaman.
The debt-collecting Clunky called sure enough at
the quoted time with 13s. and demanded receipt
for 20s. It occurred to the business man that it
would be as well to get his Chinese co-worker’s
name before he left, as he might come in useful for
future transactions. “Isay,” he asked, “what’s
j/ourname?” “Name me?” said the Chinaman,
half-way out of the door—“me Too Kwid.”

It is hard to furnish your home at Walhalla,
the Vic. township where your windows look
down the next-door chimney-flues. Some time
ago a local schoolmaster wished to put a piano
into his drawing-room. It took three days, 12
bullocks, and two men to get it from the base to
the mountain-top, and another two days to lower
it down by ropes to the level of the dominie’s
home.
