SPORTING NOTIONS.
Though St. Alban’s Wilson has nine in Melb.
Clap, it will not be known for a few weeks whether any of
them will carry his colors, Tatt.’s lottery having yet to
decide future ownership. The stable is represented by
half-a-dozen in the Caulfield Cup also.
• • ♦ *
The wealthy Mr. Gollan is the second hvrgest
nominator in Melb. Cup, having five to pay for. One
thing noticeable is the defection of the barbed-wire-tera-
pered brute, Sternchaser, whose second to Portsea in the
Australian Cup, after making the running all the way for
nis stable mate, Culloden, will ever be remembered.
Pounamu (now in M.L.) represents the stable in Stern-
chaser’s place. He is a beautiful horse, by Newminster
Beryl (full sister to Nordenfeldt, whose rather sudden
death is the turf sensation of the week).
* * * *
Mr. Sam Hordern’s celebrated race - horse
Stallion, Nordenfeldt, which has died of paralysis, con-
sequent on an accident, could, it is said, have been sold
within the last few months for at least three times the
6600 guineas paid for him by his owner. Mr. Hordern,
who has 52 youngsters by Nordenfeldt out of high-class
mares, 24 of which are believed to be again in foal to the
departed, is in treaty in England for a successor to the
dead stallion.
• • • •
The National horses doing the best work at
Flemington, so far, are Dundee and Dairaio, and the touts
at Flemington swear by them for the double. Dairaio
especially is doing splendid work, and seems to have
enough foot to win a decent fiat race.
* ♦ • •
The latest dodge of the Melb. starting-price
brigade. The owner of a horse arranges with his com-
missioners to put so much on his horse at S.P. He then
goes to the course, but stays on the flat. As his horse is not
backed inside, and as the flat prices always follow the
paideck ditto, he gets on outside at a long price, and is
sure to get good odds for the money invested in town by
his S.P. commissioners.
* * * *
Of the sires, Gozo was again the hero of the day
at Randwick on Saturday, when his offspring, Messmate
and Cradle, won the double —Duckenfield and Randwick
Stakes respectively. Gozo’s get—Pilot Boy, Sadur, Mess-
mate, and Cradle—have placed a nice little sum in stakes
to his credit during the past few weeks. What price his
next batch of yearlings?
* » • •
On dit that the most unlucky of Australian race-
horse owners is about to sell off his stud. He has raced
for years—spent thousands getting the best—yet he could
count his wins on his fingers. Several young Carbines
will be included in the list.
* * • •
“When that gilded Australian, Mr. William
Cooper, sold his horse Kirkconnel because he thought he
was inferior to Raconteur, I wrote,” remarks The Bul-
letin’s correspondent, “ that I believed he was wrong,
and that more unlikely things might happen than the
colt winning two at least of the classic races—adding that
I should not like to speak to Mr. Cooper if his discarded
horse won either the Two Thou, or Derby. What I pre-
dicted has happened. Kiikconnel easily defeated a smart
field for the Two Thousand, including the dreaded
Raconteur. Cooper’s face was a sight for the gods when
he saw the colt pass the post, and bring back to Blundell
Maple, in stakes and bets, much more than he paid for
the animal. Such bad losers as Cooper should never own
a horse. His brother, Daniel, is a much more philosophic
sportsman.”
* ♦ * •
The Yankees, Dwyer and Croker, took a nice
tittle sum out of the ring by the win of Banquet 11. in the
Two Thousand Trial Stakes at Newmarket (writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent). They don’t seem to
be particular as to price when they have a real good thing
on. The ways of Campbell, their trainer, are a puzzle to
the old-fashioned school. For instance, w'ithin half-an-
hour of Banquet 11. starting for the race named he was
given half a pail of water, and after a short rest was sent
a full-speed gallop over five furlongs. After this he was
clothed and allowed to sweat, and without even waiting
to rub him dry he was started for the race, which he won
comfortably. They took as little as 5 to 4 about him.
But they had a bad fall when their chosen one, Montauk,
which was backed heavily, was beaten. The stable com-
mission was over £6OOO. A duel between them and the
bookies can only end one way.
* * • *
It was the mysterious Mr. Granville Lloyd, of
Australia (writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent), who offered £lO,OOO for Raconteur. He in-
structed William Allison not alone to make a bid for
Raconteur, but to make an offer to Machell for a lot—-10
or 12 —of his horses, including Ravensbury. The price
asked was comparatively modest, and had itbeen genuine
business the buyer would not have had much the worst of
the deal with the 'Jubilee Stakes in view, for which
Ravensbury has a good chance.
Sir Visto’s Derby-win will resurrect British
punters’ buried faith in the “third for the Guineas.”
Lord Falmouth twice pulled off the Epsom prize with
non-favorites that had finished No. 3 in the mile race at
Newmarket, so it became for a time the proper thing for
horse-reporters to discover vast possibilities of improve-
ment in No. 3, whereas No. 1 and 2 were described as
“ thoroughly wound up” and not likely to be any filter
on Derby-day. But at length the public grew weary of
expecting animals to win a Derby for the particular reason
that they had been badly beaten a month earlier. “ Third
for the Guineas ” proved a “rotter” as often_as not.
» * * *
Judging by the times put up by the Sydney
trotters now in Maoriland, it would appear that there is
something queer about either the tracks or the watches.
* » • •
Lillie Bridge has placed a pony-trot on its pro-
gramme for Monday afternoon next, but the proprietors
have made a mistake in running it off in the afternoon.
Most of the trotting owners or riders, not being profes-
sional horsemen, have their work to attend to in the day-
time.
♦ * * ♦
Some good spins are usually seen between the
trotters coming home from Ooogee on Sunday afternoons.
Last Sunday Edith and Fairboy got together and had a
neck-and-neok mile from Randwick Course to the Zoo.
It was a great go, and a few such on the race-track in the
past might have gone a long way to popularise trotting
with the crowd.
• • • •
The Australasian points out that 13 jockeys
have been killed in the last eight months in Australia, so
far as known.
• ♦ ♦ *
A movement is on foot in England to present a
“shilling-testimonial" to W. G. Grace, and it is sug-
gested that Australia Should join in. Grace is a splendid
cricketer and a fine man, liut if Australia joins in testi-
monialising him it won’t be because he was personally
popular here. His manners were dingo-like.
* * * *
Boundary umpires were tried in Melb. for the
first time at the charity football matches. In addition to
the game being faster and more open, the central umpire
is saved much hard work by their employment, and is
better able to enforce fair play. The one disadvantage is
the expense of seven umpires as against three.
• * * •
The football-match, Paddington Electorate v.
Wallaroo, on Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday, ended
in a surprising win for Paddington by 12 points to 0.
Individually, the Wallaroos are a fine team, but as a com-
bination they are a failure. Paddington frequently ran
over them.
• • * *
Secy. Rand, of N.S.W. Rugby Union, who has
played the game for 14 years, gives up for good this
season. For long he was wing forward to the Wallaroos.
Five years ago he met with the footballer’s frequent ac-
cident—a broken collar-bone. He retires for lack of time
to train.
• * * *
The Cup offered to the Sydney footballers by
the management of Sydney Cricket Ground has to be won
three times. Randwick has scored two wins, University
one ; and as Randwick is stronger this year, it prou ises
to win the Cup right out. Whiteside, of M.L , who
•went to England with the Maori team, is one of Rand-
wick’s new players. Another Sydney team remarkably
strong this year is the Pirates.
• » ♦ »
The cycle race on Sydney cricket-ground last
Saturday brought 24 men to the front. The race was a
very fine one: the pace fast, and the riders’ judgment
particularly good. Megson rode in brilliant form, only to
finish fourth ; Williams, who had 115 yards handicap, won
by a length from Neil Matterson, who beat Phelan by a
few inches. Value of prize, £lO. The winner is captain
of the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, and is a tailor by
occupation. About two years ago he won the first
amateur cash cycle race held in Sydney—worth £4O.
* * « *
R. Lewis, on Redfern (Sydney) track last week,
put up a properly authenticated mile on his bike in 2min.
8 l-sth sec. Some idea of the pace may be gauged from
the fact that Lewis’s time is a shade of a second faster
than the trotting record held so long by the American
wonder, Maud S.
* • « •
Apparently the S. Adelaides have a mortgage-
on S.A. football premiership this year, like Essendon had
in Melb. for so long. The “Sowths” are playing with
the cream of Adelaide footballers this season, and the
only team likely to push them is the always consistent
and once mighty Norwoods.
« • « *
Cyclist Zimmerman : “ Courage, both moral
and physical, can be gained and cultivated if diligently
pursued. Courage is needed not only in racing, hut in
deciding when not to race. If, saj', a man is riding a
two-mile race and at half-a-mile from the finish the men
begin to sprint, and he finds he’s physically not strong
enough to stand the strain, he should stop; but if for
lack of courage he allows his mental powers to urge him
on in his endeavors to come in a winner, he may do him-
self great harm.” Megson, of N.S.W., needs Zim.’s
philosophy. Megson, when out of form, rode so
desperately in one race that he had to give up at last
because he simply couldn’t see where he was going. The
ground was whirling and surging under him, and before
and around him was a great blurr through which the silks
of the leading men occasionally flashed.
• * * »
That well-known pigeon -shot and all-round
sport, Mr. Fred. Gannon, travelled in the train the other
day with Geo. Garland, “ Billy ” Frost, and a few other
horsey men. Guessing the duration of a minute was the
topic, and Gannon, pulling out his chronograph, said he
didn’t think any man could guess nearer than ssecs. three
times running. Garland said he could time it to the tick,
and surprised Mr. Q. by three times guessing the minute
exactly. Frost was sitting in the corner of the carriage
next to Garland reading the evening paper, and, taking
his watch out behind the paper, without appearing to
join in the conversation, had each time with a nudge told
Garland when to stop. Gannon got out at Tempe, en-
thusiasticaboutGarland’sability as a timist. When such an
astute personage as Mr. Gannon can be so easily deceived,
who can wonder at the poor countryman being taken
down by confidence “guns.”

The sale of tickets in connection with the
distribution of the famous St. Alban’s estate is
still proceeding apace, and it is not unreasonable
to expect that the drawing will take place during
September. A couple of illustrations of the St.
Alban’s prizes are given in this issue, and “ Tat-
tersall’s ” (Mr. George Adams’) advertisement,
giving necessary business particulars of the
monster art-union, will be found elsewhere.
A Chicago doctor claims to have discovered
the microbe of death, and the question now is—
what will kill that nasty little fellow before he
kills us. The answer is probably Amykos, the
famous toilet requisite, which, by keeping the
mouth, teeth, and skin clean and sweet, gives no
chance for impurities to lodge and grow into
disease. Amykos has just obtained at Tasmanian
Exhibition the first-class certificate which it had
previously secured from every person using it.

“ House-remover ” is put up
as a regular Melb. trade.
The British Commission on
opium reports in effect that the
“awful results of the degrading
traffic ” are merely myths of the
diseased missionary imagination.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Ophthalmia is on the increase
in West Queensland, and a Bulle-
tin correspondent says that a good
eye-doctor, of temperate habits, could
soon make a fortune in the God-
forsaken Never-Never.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
It is not generally known that
the Australian Chinese have a matri-
monial agency in Hongkong, and thav
a paper is regularly issued thence
containing matrimonial advts. The
almond-eyed damsels are shipped out
in pairs—one for the importer and
one to be disposed of by public
auction.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Rev. Joseph Parker, who has
made himself notorious in England
by his avowed belief in the Scrip-
tures, urges the British Govt, to de-
clare a holy war against Turkey in
consequence of the alleged Armenian
atrocities. If Joseph ever hears the
stories knocking about as to how
nat.ves have been treated in parts of
Australia, he will probably inspire
Rosebery to bombard Sydney, Mel-
bourne, and Brisbane. But possibly,
being a Biitisher, Joseph can dis-
criminate between atrocities in
Armenia and “ healthy discipline ” in
Australia.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
To give the other and best possible side of
a somewhat lugubrious picture. J. A. Sims, late
of Rockhampton, writes from Lane’s Colonia
Cosine ; “ We are now arrived at a stage which
may be termed self-supporting. We live mainly
on beans, maudioca, maize, vegetables, and
monkey. Stewed monkey is excellent, resembling
fowl. Some of our bullocks have been sacrificed
for food. . . . Our leather, hoots and shoes,
we make ourselves. Our regular working days are
five per week, of eight hours each. We have a
newspaper, Evening Notes, which is read every
evening. Occasionally there is a little disagree-
ment, but the bitter feeling and disgraceful im-
putations of selfishness and jobbing current in
New Australia are entirely absent. We have just
finished a very enjoyable Christinas festival.”
ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE (Geelong). Interior of drawing-room.

I rotectionist Canada has discovered that
electricity, generated on the American side of
Niagara and conducted by wires for use on the
Canadian side, comes under the head of “unspeci-
fied articles ” in the tariff, and must consequently

A cold begins with inflammation of the membra-
neous lining of the intricate little passages of the
nose (which is a Hampton Court labyrinth on a
small scale). Homocea at once subdues the in-
flammation by reducing the swelling, and thereby
relieving the feeling .of “stuffiness." Moreover, it
forms a protective covering for the delicate mem-
brane, and prevents cold air, fog, or other irritating
vapour from getting at it. In influenza, Homocea
acts in the same way, and here the germ-killing
properties of the ointment further come into play.
Not much is known about the way in which influ-
enza originates, but it is quite clear that it is caused
by an invisible germ or microbe of some kind.
Homocea may,therefore, be confidently recomended
both as a cure and a preventive of influenza.

Bards of the Backblocks .
THE SANDY-WHISKERED BLOKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 and the sandy-whiskered bloke
Met close beside a backblox gate,
As neither of us had a moke
He asked me “ Would I be his mate?”
He saw my empty tucker-bag,
a “ ® etter have a feed,” says he,
And so I dropped my cursed swag
And had a welcome pint of tea.
He had some johnnies steaming hot
And mutton on a hoop-iron grill,
bays he, “ You’d better scoff the lob.”
And then, when I had scoffed my fill,
And stretched my carcase on the ground
To give my knocked-up bones a rest,
He asked “ which way I might be bound ”
“ n( i “ how things looked about the West.”
At Barker’s shanty could they tell
Of any drovers coming up,”
Because he “ had a dog to sell—
A little beauty of a pup.”
“ He’d saved, at thirty-bob a week,”
A cheque that he would try and hold,
lor he was bound to Walleye Creek
lo raise an ounce or two of gold.
He had a pound or two to spare ;
We’d “ try the diggings for a while,”
And what he had we’d gladly share
In reg’lar old colonial style,
And stand a show to make a pile.
And we could live like fighting-cocks,
For Walleye Creek was full of fish,
Great sleepy cod among the rocks—
What better could a fellow wish?
We camped beneath a Moreton ash,
And after midnight pretty late
1 woke to hear a rending crash—
The tree had fallen on my mate,
And when I tried to chop him loose,
Quite breathless with the pain, bespoke—
‘lt’s not worth while. It ain’t no use.
My legs and back and ribs is broke.
Jammed like a rat beneath a log—
The only cure’s a dose of lead.
Here, matey, take the little dog,
And take the purse beneath my head.”
He tried a dose of lead ? He did.
I saw the flash and smelt the smoke,
And then his shattered face I hid—
Good, kind old sandy-whiskered bloke!
Q-
CURLEW.
“HAVE MERCY!”—ANOTHER VERSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Oh, ’Chaser, in your onward flight,
Charge not the rails with nose in air,
But steady down and take a sight,
Shorten your stride and jump ’em fair,
Go at your fences cool and wary—
Have mercy on the man you carry.
Wild Stockhorse, mad with six months’ spell,
Don’t race through timber quite so free;
A slower pace will do as well
To put your rider up a tree.
At the blind gullies turn or tarry—
Have mercy on the man you carry.
Oh, Outlaw, on the station found
Saddled and bridled for the fray,
When once your man is on the ground
Don’t bash his brains out in your play.
Befleot! Perhaps the wretch is married—
Have mercy on the man you carried.
And, Hackney, stumbling on the road,
Go pleasanter. Hold up, you dog I
Bespect your aching flesh-galled load,
And mitigate that bumping jog ;
To nicer gaits your paces vary—
Have mercy on the man you carry.

R.A.F.
DISILLUSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
I.
’Twas in a Western “ ten by ten
A red-soil ridge with scanty trees
Bose up before me, and again
Beyond I thought I heard the seas.
I climbed and saw a sea indeed —
A sea of scrub and whitened grass;
But sea of which my soul had need,
Naught ’neath that sky of molten brass,
11.
She seemed a eirl of steadfast heart
As I gazed in her great grey eyes.
How could I tell she played a part,
And deemed me but a gilded prize ?
I loved her—and I thought I saw
Her inmost mind before me pass.
God ! when at length new-wed, I knew
Her soul was like that withered grass.
Lithgow, May, ’95.

HABET!
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Down ! And the world’s war-squadron splashes
Past, loose-reined, in the blood and the mire,
Brown arms sweep and the bared steel flashes
On to the goal of the world’s desire.
Down ! By the war-steed’s hot hoofs cowering.
Broken the sword-arm, bent the sword,
And away to the front leap the sabres showering
Blows for the hell-hearth, blows for the Lord !
Has he clutched at the moon for jewel
To bind on his bosom and wear ?
Has he fought with a Fate too cruel
And followed a face too fair ?
No matter the reason why,
He is down, and it’s little the world will care
As it sweeps in a foam-fret by.
Down ! Weeps the moon, and he never wore it,
Down ! And the stars mourn into the mist.
Fate’s red weal is across his forehead—
Somebody’s face has never been kissed !
Flushes the dawn, and one vulture-speck
Spires and spins in a reeling sky ;
Down ! And it’s little the world will reck
As it rides red-rowelled by.
N.S.W.
GLENROWAN.
During one month, no less than fire
pairs of twins (all still living) have been born in
the little township of Muswellbrook, N.S.W.
Saville Kent, in his gorgeous book on the
Great Barrier Beef, refers to clam-shells up to
10ft. broad, and the captain of an English war-
vessel claims to have seen one no less than 14ft.
across, lying under the water.
The Japanese plague has descended heavily
on California within the past three years, and last
season 10,000 of the little brown men were working
in the farms and vineyards to the displacement of
white labor. Ten years hence, if no restriction is
imposed in the interim, there are more likely to
be a hundred thousand than ten. Yet Wellington
Post demands that Maoriland should freely
throw open its ports to the Chow and Jap.,
and promises, on its own moral lOU, that they
will never even attempt to overrun the province.
1 The Vic. Naval Brigade has disbanded, the
.obsolete gun-boats are to be sold, the Nelson is
laid up apparently for good, and the old, rusted
Cerberus—which can only do knots per hour in
smooth water—has ceased to be a ship even in
name, and is permanently anchored as a “fioatiag
battery.” Therefore, Victoria has no longer a
navy, but it has a naval commandant at £1250 a
year, and a naval staff which costs about .£29,000
annually, and it will have to pay the salaries of
these potentates until their engagements expire,
and feel as well as it can about them.
THE HANDFUL OF THINGS WE KNOW
Several years ago an American humorist and poet
published some verses called “Little Breeches.”
This was an odd name given to a very small boy
who was caught out in a tremendous snowstorm,
and finally found in some hay quite a distance from
the house. However the boy got there bothered
everybody to explain. It was certain he never
could have walked. So his father said the angels
must have done it; “ they just stooped down and
toted him to where it was safe and warm,” he said.
The poetry about it (supposed to have been written
by the youngster’s father) starts off in this way :
I don’t go much on religion,
I never ain’t had no show ;
But I’ve a middling tight grip, sir,
On the handful of things I know.
That’s it ; the handful of things we know. There
aren’t many of ’em, but there are a few. And one
of them is this : That for a hundred results there
is only one cause. Nature developes and makes
differences; never a new force.
Here, for example, is an incident which shows
our meaning. About Christmas, 1889, Mr. E. B.
Wright had an attack of influenza. Previous to
this he had always been strong and hearty. Well
he got over the influenza; still, it had given him
(as he says) “a shake.” After this he got along
fairly Well, until February,of this year (1892) when
the influenza attacked him again. This time the
malady “meant business.” Nearly every bone
and muscle in his body ached like sore teeth. His
skin was hot and dry, and to bed he was obliged
to go. For sixteen days he was under a doctor.
At the end of that time he found himself alive
and that was about all you could say for him.
In his letter he goes on to tell what happened
next. “I had a foul taste in the mouth,” he says,
“and my teeth and tongue were covered with a
thick slimy phlegm. My wife says my tongue
was like an oyster-shell, and I’m sure it was rough
as a nutmeg grater. What I ate, which wasn’t
much, gave me pain in the chest and sides. After
a mouthful or two I felt full and blown out, and
I used to swell to a great size. By-and-by a
hacking cough set in and my breathing got short
and quick. At night I lay for hours gasping for
breath, and often coughed so I was afraid I should
burst a blood vessel. I got weaker and weaker and
was like a broken-ivinded horse. The doctor said
it was asthma, but he wasn’t able to relieve it.
Although I live only two minutes’ walk from the
factory where I work, 1 had to stop and rest on
my way many a time.
“Thus matters went with me until June, 1892.
Then one day I took up the Essex Newsman, and
read of a man living at Barsham, near Bungay,
having been cured by Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. I got some of this medicine from, the
International Tea Company, Braintree. After a
few doses my breathing grew easier, and by
keeping on with the Syrup my food soon digested,
the cough left me, and I gained strength. lam
now as strong as ever, can eat anything, and walk
for miles. lam a brushmaker, and work at the
factory of Messrs. John West and Sons, High
Street, Braintree, and have lived in this town over
forty years. (Signed) E. B. Weight, Sandpit
Hoad, Braintree, Essex, August 23rd, 1892.”
Now let us see how this illustrates the proposi-
tion we started out with. For almost three years
Mr. Wright was ill with what seemed like a series
of different diseases. He had the influenza twice,
the asthma once, and another disease which he
gives no name to—even if he recognised it. Look
for a moment at the variety and incongruity of the
pains and troubles he mentions, and he doesn’t
describe them all, either. You would fancy he
had half a dozen ailments at least. Yet he had
but one— indigestion and dyspepsia—of which all
his bodily disturbances (influenza included—a
blood disease) were symptoms. All came out of
the stomach, and when Seigel’s Syrup set that
right the others quietly departed.
What, then, is one thing of “ the handful of
things we know ?” Answer: That nearly all
sorts of diseases are really symptoms of indigestion
and dyspepsia, and that Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup cures it.
Double that fact up in your fist and hold on to
It tight.

An Australian at ’Ome :—Last Sunday, at
Hampton Court, on the Thames, I was treated to
a strange and unaccustomed sight. Two insipid-
scented johnnies (one eye-glassed) lolled in the
stern of a heavy 20ft. boat, while one of the
brave-and-free—a poor devil of a laborer, with a
pallid face—laboriously dragged them along from
the bank. The tugging of the line round his
shoulders had chafed his ragged shirt to his skin,
and when, at the end of a long, heart-breaking
drag, the free-born human mule was stopped,
the scented sons of a generous race rewarded him
with —a shilling.— Dryblo web.

Graceful, tasteful, saucy, witty;
There’s little that she cannot do
From algebra to Irish stew :
She cycles, boxes, fiddles, flirts,
Makes poems, cheese and model shirts.
You think she can do all things ?, Hush !
She can do everything but blush.

The Westralian Gold-Boom, — In a
W.A. Pawnshop,
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Time, 9 o’clock, Saturday evening. Behind the
counter, a fair-haired chubby-faced Australian
boy. The proprietor, an Israelite with 100 per
shunt written all over his face, sits on a chair in a
room off the shop.
Enter a poor, haggard-looking, shabbily-dressed
woman with one child in her arms and two other
children, poorly-clad, trailing on to her shirts.
Boy: “You here again! You live in this
shop. ”
Woman: “Yds; haven’t had a word from my
husband yet, although he has been gone these
three months to Ooolgardie.”
Boy (cheerfully): “Ah! perhaps he’s dead.
Lots of fever up there.”
Woman; “For God’s sake, don’t say that!
He’s a good husband when sober, and, when he
dies, may the Lord grant that my three children
and myself die also ! We would starve to death
if we didn’t.” (Cries).
Mr. Mosenstein : “Don’t chry here, my goot
vouwn? Spoils my bithness. Veil, vot have you
got thith time? Hey !”
Woman : “ The only thing left me in the world’s
my wedding-ring. There’s nothing in the house,
the bailiff’s got all my furniture, and you’ve got
the family Bible, my jewellery, and all the chil-
dren’s, and my Sunday clothes and boots.”
Mr. M. : “Veil, let me thee the ring 1”
She (pulling it off and handing it to him): “ It’s
enough to make my poor mother turn in her
grave to see her only child pawning her marriage-
ring. It oost £3 when new.”
Mr. M. : “That’s 20 years ago.”
She (sharply); “ Indeed it’s only seven. I’m
only 25.”
Mr. M, : “Yell, you look 50! Vot you vaut
on it?”
She; “A pound.”
Mr. M. : “A pound 1 Do you vant to ruin
me? I’ll give you three bob. Not a penny
more.”
She: “Say five shillings to keep us from starving
for another week. I’m sure to hear then from my
husband, and will pay all back.”
Mr. M. i “ No.”
Then she begins to coax him, asking him “ how
he would like to see his wife in such a position,”
but, of course, he couldn’t imagine such a thing.
No one thoroughly seized of the beauty and the
glory of the compound-interest table ever could.
She snatches up the three shillings and walks
quickly out of the shop, one of the children re-
marking : “Mother, you’re not going to let that
nasty old man keep your pretty ring. What
would father say 1 ”
Then there enters the shop a travel-stained
digger with a swag. His clothes are studded with
stains of red dust, his boots burst in all directions.
His hands and face are badly mosquito bitten, his
eyes almost closed with sandy blight. Evidently
just finished a tramp from Ooolgardie.
Digger, to boy : “ Matey, for Christ’s sake lend
me ten bob on this swag till Monday, when I hope
to get a bit.”
Mr. M. : “ Vy do you insult us ven you vant to
porro monish from us ? Another swag 1 That
ith the fortieth I have lent on this very night. All
Ooolgardie vath coming to my shop this ev’nin’.
All I can lend is two thillings.”
Digger ; “ How can a chap carry througli Sun-
day »n two bob? Make it five shillings. It oost
me a couple o’ notes, and it’s quite new.”
Mr. M. ; “You vas pawning now, not buying.
Take two thillings or leave them.”
He took them.

Archbishop Carr gives the A rgus an ex-
planation of what the Pope means by indulgences
to the faithful who pray for England’s conver-
sion. It just shows the impossibility of any
accommodation between the Vatican and common
sense. The Archbishop proves that the Pope has
an incalculable stock of works of supererogation
done by the saints of the past, a sort of holy bank
on which he is empowered to draw cheques to any
figure.
At Ararat (Vic.), during the periodical raid
on the Chinese camp, when the usual Celestials’
concubine is seized and charged with drunkenness,
language and vagrancy, and when the usual num-
ber of mean Europeans turn up to swear they are
supporting her, the police-sergeant was questioning
a female witness. “ What’s your name?” “Mrs.
Ah Fat,” she replied. “ Are you quite sure you’re
name is Mrs. Ah Fat?” “Well,” she replied,
tearfully, “T think I deserve it after living with a
man for 20 years.” Even the sergeant was con-
vinced by this unanswerable appeal.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Melb. Pressman ” is
right in his comparison of R.O. and Anglican
clergy. I once sought a Balmain Anglican parson
for press information, and got a four-line para-
graph. Afterwards, he requested me to remain,
and I had visions of soda-water and other horrors,
but resigned myself to my fate. “Do you live
in Balmain ?” he asked. I told him I lived at
Bondi (which was a lie, but near enough). “ Oh,”
he sighed, “you see I am forming a Bible-class
for young men, and thought perhaps you would
join. Er—good afternoon.” Missionising the
heathen pressman 1 That afternoon I attended a
R.O. school festival, and four of us benighted
scribes were afterwards regaled convincingly by
the priests. We agreed to give the R.O.’s half-a-
column.—Thokold D.
A certain Melbourne pawnbroker has lost
all respect for wandering minstrels who blow wind
instruments. His name is Mossy Aarons, and
some time ago a German-Jewish gentleman came
into the shop with a tale of misery terminating in
a pathetic appeal for the loan of a saxhorn from the
stock. Armed with a saxhorn he would, he said,
be able to touch the heart of the people and earn
money. So Mr. Aarons allowed the stranger to
borrow the article, on condition that he should
not blow far away, but look in from time to time
to report progress. Unfortunately, a saxhorn
soloist finds it hard to remain at one spot. By
the time this particular artist had played his third
tune he had received ten successive pennies to go
away, so at last he went away—with the sax-
horn. In due course he got a month’s imprison-
ment for larceny as a bailee ; but what is that to a
pawnbroker’s shattered philanthropy ? .
Jack Denne’s Pupil .
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Everybody in that Maoriland district knew Jack
Denne. He was married to squatter Nelson’s
only daughter, Cora, and had a billet as sub-
manager on the station.
Djnne had been keeping square for four or five
years, an unusual thing for him.
Old Nelson thought a lot of Jack, and used to
talk about the way be had pulled himself together.
Jack began to tire of the quiet station life, and
frequently took trips to the nearest township, with
the result that latterly he invariably returned
home drunk.
Nelson stood it awhile, but Jack’s trips became
so frequent that he at last told him that if he did
not pull up he would have to leave the station.
Jack thought the old man was only talking, be-
cause if he went he would take hi wife away with
him. Nelson had thought of this, and had got
Cora’s promise that if he sent Jack away, for a
time, she woqld not leave the homestead. They
said to each other that, in a few months’ time, if
Jack squared up, they would take him back.
One night Jack returned home after a two-days’
spree. Nelson packed him off, and as Cora had
been sent away previously for a month’s holiday,
Jack did not see her before he left.
Jack wandered about the country, shearing in
the summer and bush-felling in the winter, and
when work was slack he lived with the Maoris at
the pah at Kororo. Being a good Maori speaker,
he soon took up with a Maori wahine, called
Mutu, the daughter of an old chief.
Jack taught her to English, and when he
was away in the township at nights drinking she
used to amuse herself by learning to read f She
had got hold of some of his letters that he had left
lying about the whare.
He had received one from his wife, Cora, and it
was left lying with the rest. She told him in it
that her father was willing to give him another
chance, and asked him to come home on Christ-
mas Day. Mutu read the letter as well as she
could, and knowing it was not long till Christinas,
she thought Jack might go home to his wife with-
out returning to the pah.
She put on her flax slippers and went down to
the township to bring him home.
Jack always said, when he was drunk, that he
had made a mistake in teaching her English.
A short cut to the pah led right to the face of
a cliff. An arm of the sea ran up a narrow gorge,
where fishermen set their nets, as the schnapper
and kawhai came up through the gorge with the
tide to feed. Straight along this track to within
a few yards of the cliff —then a sharp turn to the
left brought you to Jack’s whare.
Mutu led Jack along. A part of Cora’s letter,
“ Come home on Xmas day,” flashed through her
mind. Would she let him go ? Thinking how the
other Maoris in the pah would talk if he left her,
she led him on.
The fishermen hauled in their nets in the early
morning. They found a few fish, and the bodies
of Jaok and Mutu.
Education is a failure.

Sample of wild humor from S.M. Herald :
Commander M'Giffen had some very narrow escapes
during the Yalu battle. At one time he was directly in
front of the mouth of a 12in. gun on his ship as the
gunner was swinging it into place to fire. He closed his
eyes and waited for certain death. He opened them in a
few seconds and rolled off the superstructure just before
the gun went off.
The man who waited a few seconds under such
conditions would stop to read an article on bi-
metallism while he was running away from a tiger.

mand-in-Ohief has been revived. The idea now
is a Commission to replace him. The spectacle of

supreme command of an army is not less ludicrous
than would be the appointment of Martin Tupper
as successor to Chancellor Bismarck. Neverthe-

the Royal Family.
Is the “person named Rogers” dead yet?
His spirit still inspires that amusing “Colonial
office ” about which Higinbotham said something.
The “Colonial office ” taxes Hongkong and Singa-
pore for the protection of “British trade” in
the East, just as Australia is taxed (voluntarily)
for defence of “ British trade ” here. Trade
follows the flag, and the beastly colonists pay for
the protection of it. The blackmail now levied on
Singapore is £lOO,OOO per annum, and the wretched
place has received notice that after 1896 it will be
£120,000. Newfoundland proposes to submit
itself to the Colonial office —and, as Singapore
shows signs of disputing the bill, perhaps New-
foundland will have to make up the Singapore
deficit as well as its own.
Mr. Eagleson, a Vic. barrister, has just
written a book on “Employers’ Liability,” de-
scribing well that wretched British-born, Yankee-
nurtured principle, used so long and so effectively
by employers to bludgeon maimed employes and
their relatives, and known as the Common
Employment doctrine. The writer shows to
demonstration that the Employers’ Liability Acts
have not so completely extinguished the doctrine
as workmen and their legislators imagine. It
would do Labor-members no harm to read this
part of the book, as fruitful in suggestions of
flaws in the Acts. The Victorian Act is only a
temporary one, and workmen will wake up some
fine morning after a colliery-accident to find their
Act lapsed and themselves thrown on the tender
mercies of the common law. The book is full of
references to N.S.W. cases. The hand of a demo-
crat is visible in the index. The words “ master ”
and “servant’’are relegated to the obscurity of
cross-references, and Higinbotham’s initiative fol-
lowed of “employer” and The
index is a perfect one from every standpoint.
